beginner

Definition and Components of Jadal
Define jadal and its innate components using everyday examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three key components of jadal (istifsar, man', naqd) as defined by al-Tufi, and distinguish between valid objection (man') and obstinacy (mukabara).

Core Idea
Jadal is an innate craft with three operational components: asking, objecting, and counterexample.

In this first lesson, we explore the foundational definition of jadal as a craft nearly innate in humans. Al-Tufi's observation of children and common people reveals the structure of dialectical exchange. We will dissect the three components that make up every jadal encounter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Read the Arabic passage and note that the author introduces three technical terms: istifsar, man' (also called mukabara), and naqd. These are the building blocks of jadal.
The author equates man' with mukabara in this passage, but later usage may differentiate them.
2
Examine istifsar (asking for clarification)
Find the examples of istifsar: 'What did you say?', 'How did you say it?', 'What does this mean?'. Recognize that this is a neutral, inquiry-based component.
3
Examine man' and mukabara (objection/obstinacy)
The author gives an example: 'By Allah, it is not so, it is only such and such.' This is a blunt denial without evidence. Understand that man' here is synonymous with mukabara.
In later usul al-fiqh, man' becomes a technical term for a valid objection with evidence.
4
Examine naqd (counterexample)
Study the amulet example: one person claims an amulet prevented sickness; the other provides a counterexample where an amulet did not prevent sickness. This is naqd: existence of cause without effect.
Naqd is a powerful refutation tool in Islamic legal reasoning.
5
Reflect on children's intuitive logic
The author says children understand that opposition invalidates a proof and that persisting with a claim after opposition is 'tarjih bi-la murajjih' (preference without preponderator). Recognize this as a logical fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل صناعة تكاد تكون فطرية، إن لم تكن كذلك حقيقة، فإنا نرى العامة، بل الصبيان، تقع بينهم المناظرات على القانون الصناعي، من إيراد الاستفسار، كقول أحدهم لآخر : أيش قلت؟ أو : كيف قلت؟ أو : أيش معنى هذا؟ والمنع هو الذي يسمونه المكابرة، نحو قولهم : والله ما كذا، وما هو إلا كذا، والنقض كقول بعضهم : فلان علق على دابته عوذة فما مرضت فيقول الآخر : قد علق فلان على دابته عوذة فماتت أو مرضت وهو وجود العلة بدون الحكم وهو النقض. والصبيان ببدائههم يدركون أن المعارضة تبطل الحجة، وأنها بعد المعارضة ترجيح بلا مرجح وأنه باطل، فإذا افتخر أحدهم على باقيهم بطفر نهر أو وثوب جدار أو نحو ذلك، اجتهدوا على أن يفعلوا مثل فعله، فإن صح لهم علموا أن افتخاره عليهم قد بطل وأنه صار ترجيحا بلا مرجح، وأن ذلك باطل، وأشباه ذلك مما يقع من العامة كثيرًا. والحقيقة أن الجدل ليس ظاهرة مقصورة على الإنسان دون غيره من مخلوقات في هذا الكون، بل إنه وجد حتى قبل وجود الإنسان ذاته، فقد سأل الملائكة عن المغزى من خلق الإنسان وأبدوا اندهاشهم من خلق كائن سيفسد في الأرض ويهلك الحرث والنسل، وقد نقل القرآن الكريم في مواضع متعددة قصة الحوار الجدلي الذي دار بين الله عز وجل وملائكته، فقال سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ قَالَ رَبُّكَ لِلْمَلائِكَةِ إِنِّي جَاعِلٌ فِي الْأَرْضِ خَلِيفَةٌ قَالُوا أَتَجْعَلُ فِيهَا مَن يُفْسِدُ فِيهَا وَيَسْفِكُ الدِّمَاءَ وَنَحْنُ نُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِكَ وَنُقَدِّسُ لَكَ قَالَ إِنِّي أَعْلَمُ مَا لَا تَعْلَمُونَ وَعَلَّمَ آدَمَ الأَسْمَاءَ كُلَّهَا ثُمَّ عَرَضَهُمْ عَلَى الْمَلائِكَةِ فَقَالَ أَنْبِئُونِي بِأَسْمَاءِ هَؤُلَاءِ إِن كُنتُمْ صَادِقِينَ قَالُوا سُبْحَانَكَ لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْعَلِيمُ الْحَكِيمُ قَالَ يَا آدَمُ أَنْبِئْهُم بِأَسْمَائِهِمْ فَلَمَّا أَنْبَأَهُم بِأَسْمَائِهِمْ قَالَ أَلَمْ أَقُل لَّكُمْ إِنِّي أَعْلَمُ غَيْبَ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَأَعْلَمُ مَا تُبْدُونَ وَمَا كُنْتُمْ تَكْتُمُونَ) لقد جادل الملائكة في خلق آدم جدل استفهام وطلب تعلم، ولكن إبليس جادل فيه مكابرة ومعاندة، وذلك حين أمره الله سبحانه وتعالى بالسجود لآدم تكريما له وتشريفا، فرفض الامتثال بدعوى أنه أفضل من آدم، واستدل لأفضليته المزعومة بأنه هو مخلوق من نار، وأما آدم فمخلوق من طين، والنار - في نظره القاصر - أفضل من الطين . قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَاكُمْ ثُمَّ صَوَّرْنَاكُمْ ثُمَّ قُلْنَا لِلْمَلَائِكَةِ اسْجُدُوا لِآدَمَ فَسَجَدُوا إِلَّا إِبْلِيسَ لَمْ يَكُن مِّنَ السَّاجِدِينَ قَالَ مَا مَنَعَكَ أَلَّا تَسْجُدَ إِذْ أَمَرْتُكَ قَالَ أَنَا خَيْرٌ مِّنْهُ خَلَقْتَنِي مِن نَّارٍ وَخَلَقْتَهُ مِن طِينٍ قَالَ فَاهْبِطْ مِنْهَا فَمَا يَكُونُ لَكَ أَن تَتَكَبَّرَ فِيهَا فَاخْرُجْ إِنَّكَ مِنَ الصَّاغِرِينَ قَالَ أَنظِرْنِي إِلَى يَوْمِ يُبْعَثُونَ قَالَ إِنَّكَ مِنَ الْمُنظَرِينَ قَالَ فَبِمَا أَغْوَيْتَنِي لَأَقْعُدَنَّ لَهُمْ صِرَاطَكَ الْمُسْتَقِيمَ ثُمَّ لَآتِيَنَّهُم مِّن بَيْنِ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَمِنْ خَلْفِهِمْ وَعَنْ أَيْمَانِهِمْ وَعَن شَمَائِلِهِمْ وَلَا تَجِدُ أَكْثَرَهُمْ شَاكِرِينَ قَالَ اخْرُجْ مِنْهَا مَذْءُومًا مَّدْحُورًا لَّمَن تَبِعَكَ مِنْهُمْ لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ مِنكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ) وهكذا يتبين أن الجدل ظاهرة كونية وجدت حتى قبل وجود الإنسان، ثم ارتبطت بوجوده في هذه الأرض، وأصبحت طبيعة من طبائعه الفطرية التي لا ينفك عنها في سائر أحواله وفي أنشطته المتنوعة في هذه الحياة.
Dialectic is a craft that is almost innate, if not actually so, for we see common people, even children, engaging in debates according to the technical rules, such as asking for clarification, like one saying to another: 'What did you say?' or 'How did you say it?' or 'What does this mean?' And objection (al-man') is what they call obstinacy (al-mukābara), like their saying: 'By Allah, it is not so, it is only such and such.' And counterexample (al-naqd) is like one saying: 'So-and-so hung an amulet on his animal and it did not get sick,' and the other says: 'So-and-so hung an amulet on his animal and it died or got sick'—that is the existence of the cause without the effect, and that is al-naqd. And children, with their innate dispositions, realize that opposition invalidates the proof, and that after opposition there is a preponderance without a preponderator, and that is invalid. So if one of them boasts over the rest by jumping over a river or climbing a wall or the like, they strive to do the same as he did. If they succeed, they know that his boasting over them has been invalidated, and that it became a preponderance without a preponderator, and that is invalid. And similar things occur frequently among common people. And the truth is that dialectic is not a phenomenon confined to humans apart from other creatures in this universe, rather it existed even before the existence of humans themselves. For the angels asked about the purpose of creating humans and expressed astonishment at the creation of a being that would cause corruption on earth and shed blood, and the noble Qur'an has transmitted in multiple places the story of the dialectical dialogue that took place between Allah Almighty and His angels. He, exalted be He, said: [Qur'an 2:30-33] And indeed the angels debated concerning the creation of Adam with a debate of inquiry and seeking knowledge, but Iblis debated with obstinacy and defiance, when Allah Almighty commanded him to prostrate to Adam as an honor and distinction, so he refused to comply, claiming that he was better than Adam, and he argued for his alleged superiority by saying that he was created from fire, while Adam was created from clay, and fire—in his deficient view—is better than clay. He, exalted be He, said: [Qur'an 7:11-18] Thus it becomes clear that dialectic is a universal phenomenon that existed even before the existence of humans, then it became linked to human existence on this earth, and became a part of their innate nature from which they are never free in all their states and in their various activities in this life.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of jadal (dialectic) by first characterizing it as an almost innate craft (firriyya). The author, quoting Imam Najm al-Din al-Tufi, observes that even common people and children engage in dialectical exchanges following technical rules. Three key components of such exchanges are identified: al-istifsar (asking for clarification), al-man' (objection, also called mukabara or obstinacy), and al-naqd (counterexample). The author then illustrates how children intuitively understand that a valid objection nullifies a proof, and that continuing to claim superiority after a challenge is a 'preponderance without a preponderator,' which is logically invalid. The passage then expands the scope of jadal beyond humans, arguing that it is a universal phenomenon that predates humanity. Evidence is drawn from the Qur'an: the angels' questioning of Allah's decision to create Adam (a dialectical inquiry seeking understanding) and Iblis's argument against prostrating to Adam (an arrogant, obstinate debate). The angels' dialogue is characterized as 'jadal istifham wa talab ta'allum' (debate of inquiry and seeking knowledge), while Iblis's is 'mukabara wa mu'anada' (obstinacy and defiance). The passage concludes that jadal is an inherent, inescapable part of human nature, rooted in the very fabric of existence.

الاستفسار

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between 'man'' and 'mukabara'?
A) They are opposites.
B) Man' is a type of mukabara.
C) They are synonymous in this text.
D) Mukabara is a type of man'.

Key Takeaway
Jadal is built on three intuitive components: asking for clarification, objecting, and providing counterexamples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following exchange: 'Why is riba forbidden? Because it involves unfair gain.' Your opponent objects: 'But what about profit from trade? That also involves gain but is permitted.' Identify which component of jadal this objection represents (istifsar, man', or naqd). Justify your answer using the definitions from the lesson.
?
Observe a real-life debate (or recall one) and list an instance of istifsar, man', and naqd. Write them down with brief explanations.

intermediate
10 min
Jadal as a Universal Phenomenon: Quranic Precedents
Expand definition of jadal as universal phenomenon using Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between constructive jadal (istifham) and destructive jadal (mukabara) using the Quranic examples of the angels and Iblis.

Core Idea
Jadal is not only human; the Quran shows its earliest forms in angelic inquiry and satanic obstinacy.

The author expands the scope of jadal beyond humanity by citing the Quran. The angels' debate about Adam's creation was a sincere request for knowledge, while Iblis's refusal to prostrate was an arrogant argument based on a false premise. This lesson examines these two types and their outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Note the claim: jadal predates humans
Read the part where the author says jadal existed before humans. This is a strong claim that the rest of the passage proves with Quranic stories.
2
Examine the angels' dialogue (Quran 2:30-33)
Identify that the angels asked a question: 'أَتَجْعَلُ فِيهَا مَن يُفْسِدُ فِيهَا وَيَسْفِكُ الدِّمَاءَ؟' This is a form of istifsar (seeking clarification). They were not rebellious but curious.
The author calls this 'jadal istifham wa talab ta'allum' (debate of inquiry and seeking knowledge).
3
Contrast with Iblis's dialogue (Quran 7:11-18)
Iblis's argument: 'أَنَا خَيْرٌ مِّنْهُ خَلَقْتَنِي مِن نَّارٍ وَخَلَقْتَهُ مِن طِينٍ' This is mukabara: obstinacy and defiance. He uses a weak analogy to justify disobedience.
4
Identify the fallacy in Iblis's reasoning
Iblis uses an analogical argument: fire is better than clay, so I am better than Adam. This is a false analogy (qiyas fasad). The premise that fire is better than clay is not universally true, and even if it were, it does not justify refusing a divine command.
This is a classic example of tarjih bi-la murajjih: asserting superiority without a valid preponderator.
5
Conclude that jadal is innate and universal
The author concludes that jadal is inherent in creation, appearing in both angels (constructive) and Iblis (destructive). It is a natural part of human and cosmic existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل صناعة تكاد تكون فطرية، إن لم تكن كذلك حقيقة، فإنا نرى العامة، بل الصبيان، تقع بينهم المناظرات على القانون الصناعي، من إيراد الاستفسار، كقول أحدهم لآخر : أيش قلت؟ أو : كيف قلت؟ أو : أيش معنى هذا؟ والمنع هو الذي يسمونه المكابرة، نحو قولهم : والله ما كذا، وما هو إلا كذا، والنقض كقول بعضهم : فلان علق على دابته عوذة فما مرضت فيقول الآخر : قد علق فلان على دابته عوذة فماتت أو مرضت وهو وجود العلة بدون الحكم وهو النقض. والصبيان ببدائههم يدركون أن المعارضة تبطل الحجة، وأنها بعد المعارضة ترجيح بلا مرجح وأنه باطل، فإذا افتخر أحدهم على باقيهم بطفر نهر أو وثوب جدار أو نحو ذلك، اجتهدوا على أن يفعلوا مثل فعله، فإن صح لهم علموا أن افتخاره عليهم قد بطل وأنه صار ترجيحا بلا مرجح، وأن ذلك باطل، وأشباه ذلك مما يقع من العامة كثيرًا. والحقيقة أن الجدل ليس ظاهرة مقصورة على الإنسان دون غيره من مخلوقات في هذا الكون، بل إنه وجد حتى قبل وجود الإنسان ذاته، فقد سأل الملائكة عن المغزى من خلق الإنسان وأبدوا اندهاشهم من خلق كائن سيفسد في الأرض ويهلك الحرث والنسل، وقد نقل القرآن الكريم في مواضع متعددة قصة الحوار الجدلي الذي دار بين الله عز وجل وملائكته، فقال سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ قَالَ رَبُّكَ لِلْمَلائِكَةِ إِنِّي جَاعِلٌ فِي الْأَرْضِ خَلِيفَةٌ قَالُوا أَتَجْعَلُ فِيهَا مَن يُفْسِدُ فِيهَا وَيَسْفِكُ الدِّمَاءَ وَنَحْنُ نُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِكَ وَنُقَدِّسُ لَكَ قَالَ إِنِّي أَعْلَمُ مَا لَا تَعْلَمُونَ وَعَلَّمَ آدَمَ الأَسْمَاءَ كُلَّهَا ثُمَّ عَرَضَهُمْ عَلَى الْمَلائِكَةِ فَقَالَ أَنْبِئُونِي بِأَسْمَاءِ هَؤُلَاءِ إِن كُنتُمْ صَادِقِينَ قَالُوا سُبْحَانَكَ لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْعَلِيمُ الْحَكِيمُ قَالَ يَا آدَمُ أَنْبِئْهُم بِأَسْمَائِهِمْ فَلَمَّا أَنْبَأَهُم بِأَسْمَائِهِمْ قَالَ أَلَمْ أَقُل لَّكُمْ إِنِّي أَعْلَمُ غَيْبَ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَأَعْلَمُ مَا تُبْدُونَ وَمَا كُنْتُمْ تَكْتُمُونَ) لقد جادل الملائكة في خلق آدم جدل استفهام وطلب تعلم، ولكن إبليس جادل فيه مكابرة ومعاندة، وذلك حين أمره الله سبحانه وتعالى بالسجود لآدم تكريما له وتشريفا، فرفض الامتثال بدعوى أنه أفضل من آدم، واستدل لأفضليته المزعومة بأنه هو مخلوق من نار، وأما آدم فمخلوق من طين، والنار - في نظره القاصر - أفضل من الطين . قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَاكُمْ ثُمَّ صَوَّرْنَاكُمْ ثُمَّ قُلْنَا لِلْمَلَائِكَةِ اسْجُدُوا لِآدَمَ فَسَجَدُوا إِلَّا إِبْلِيسَ لَمْ يَكُن مِّنَ السَّاجِدِينَ قَالَ مَا مَنَعَكَ أَلَّا تَسْجُدَ إِذْ أَمَرْتُكَ قَالَ أَنَا خَيْرٌ مِّنْهُ خَلَقْتَنِي مِن نَّارٍ وَخَلَقْتَهُ مِن طِينٍ قَالَ فَاهْبِطْ مِنْهَا فَمَا يَكُونُ لَكَ أَن تَتَكَبَّرَ فِيهَا فَاخْرُجْ إِنَّكَ مِنَ الصَّاغِرِينَ قَالَ أَنظِرْنِي إِلَى يَوْمِ يُبْعَثُونَ قَالَ إِنَّكَ مِنَ الْمُنظَرِينَ قَالَ فَبِمَا أَغْوَيْتَنِي لَأَقْعُدَنَّ لَهُمْ صِرَاطَكَ الْمُسْتَقِيمَ ثُمَّ لَآتِيَنَّهُم مِّن بَيْنِ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَمِنْ خَلْفِهِمْ وَعَنْ أَيْمَانِهِمْ وَعَن شَمَائِلِهِمْ وَلَا تَجِدُ أَكْثَرَهُمْ شَاكِرِينَ قَالَ اخْرُجْ مِنْهَا مَذْءُومًا مَّدْحُورًا لَّمَن تَبِعَكَ مِنْهُمْ لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ مِنكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ) وهكذا يتبين أن الجدل ظاهرة كونية وجدت حتى قبل وجود الإنسان، ثم ارتبطت بوجوده في هذه الأرض، وأصبحت طبيعة من طبائعه الفطرية التي لا ينفك عنها في سائر أحواله وفي أنشطته المتنوعة في هذه الحياة.
Dialectic is a craft that is almost innate, if not actually so, for we see common people, even children, engaging in debates according to the technical rules, such as asking for clarification, like one saying to another: 'What did you say?' or 'How did you say it?' or 'What does this mean?' And objection (al-man') is what they call obstinacy (al-mukābara), like their saying: 'By Allah, it is not so, it is only such and such.' And counterexample (al-naqd) is like one saying: 'So-and-so hung an amulet on his animal and it did not get sick,' and the other says: 'So-and-so hung an amulet on his animal and it died or got sick'—that is the existence of the cause without the effect, and that is al-naqd. And children, with their innate dispositions, realize that opposition invalidates the proof, and that after opposition there is a preponderance without a preponderator, and that is invalid. So if one of them boasts over the rest by jumping over a river or climbing a wall or the like, they strive to do the same as he did. If they succeed, they know that his boasting over them has been invalidated, and that it became a preponderance without a preponderator, and that is invalid. And similar things occur frequently among common people. And the truth is that dialectic is not a phenomenon confined to humans apart from other creatures in this universe, rather it existed even before the existence of humans themselves. For the angels asked about the purpose of creating humans and expressed astonishment at the creation of a being that would cause corruption on earth and shed blood, and the noble Qur'an has transmitted in multiple places the story of the dialectical dialogue that took place between Allah Almighty and His angels. He, exalted be He, said: [Qur'an 2:30-33] And indeed the angels debated concerning the creation of Adam with a debate of inquiry and seeking knowledge, but Iblis debated with obstinacy and defiance, when Allah Almighty commanded him to prostrate to Adam as an honor and distinction, so he refused to comply, claiming that he was better than Adam, and he argued for his alleged superiority by saying that he was created from fire, while Adam was created from clay, and fire—in his deficient view—is better than clay. He, exalted be He, said: [Qur'an 7:11-18] Thus it becomes clear that dialectic is a universal phenomenon that existed even before the existence of humans, then it became linked to human existence on this earth, and became a part of their innate nature from which they are never free in all their states and in their various activities in this life.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of jadal (dialectic) by first characterizing it as an almost innate craft (firriyya). The author, quoting Imam Najm al-Din al-Tufi, observes that even common people and children engage in dialectical exchanges following technical rules. Three key components of such exchanges are identified: al-istifsar (asking for clarification), al-man' (objection, also called mukabara or obstinacy), and al-naqd (counterexample). The author then illustrates how children intuitively understand that a valid objection nullifies a proof, and that continuing to claim superiority after a challenge is a 'preponderance without a preponderator,' which is logically invalid. The passage then expands the scope of jadal beyond humans, arguing that it is a universal phenomenon that predates humanity. Evidence is drawn from the Qur'an: the angels' questioning of Allah's decision to create Adam (a dialectical inquiry seeking understanding) and Iblis's argument against prostrating to Adam (an arrogant, obstinate debate). The angels' dialogue is characterized as 'jadal istifham wa talab ta'allum' (debate of inquiry and seeking knowledge), while Iblis's is 'mukabara wa mu'anada' (obstinacy and defiance). The passage concludes that jadal is an inherent, inescapable part of human nature, rooted in the very fabric of existence.

Key Terms
الجدل
الاستفسار
المكابرة
المعاندة
قياس فاسد

Checkpoint

A) Inquiry and seeking knowledge (istifham)
B) Obstinacy and defiance (mukabara)
C) Asking for clarification (istifsar)
D) Counterexample (naqd)

Key Takeaway
Jadal can be constructive (istifham) or destructive (mukabara); the Quran provides archetypes for both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the angels' question in Surah al-Baqara: 'أَتَجْعَلُ فِيهَا مَن يُفْسِدُ فِيهَا وَيَسْفِكُ الدِّمَاءَ' Is this istifsar or mukabara? Support your answer with the definitions from Lesson 1.

Sahaba Disagreement on Grandfather's Inheritance
Author presents the dispute among Sahaba regarding the grandfather's inheritance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two opposing positions in the Sahaba debate on grandfather inheritance and explain how dialectical resolution led to the principle of tashrik.

Core Idea
Dialectical debate among the Sahaba resolved a major inheritance dispute through mutual retraction and consensus.

This passage recounts a famous disagreement among the Companions on whether the grandfather excludes siblings from inheritance. Through dialectical exchange, they reached a shared principle of tashrik. Understanding how they argued and resolved is a model for ijtihad in Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
List the Companions who held that the grandfather excludes siblings (like the father) and those who held that he shares with siblings. Note the key figures: Ibn Abbas, Ibn Umar, Aisha vs. Ali, Zayd, Ibn Mas'ud.
The passage names specific individuals for each side.
2
Extract the rationale for each view
The first group analogizes the grandfather to the father; the second group argues that the term 'father' in the Qur'an is metaphorical when applied to the grandfather.
This shows the role of linguistic analysis and analogy (qiyas) in their reasoning.
3
Trace Umar's evolving opinion
Umar initially agreed with Abu Bakr but after debating with Zayd and Ali, he expressed regret and later retracted. Note his statement: 'how can he be a son to me while I am not a father to him?'
Umar's change illustrates the openness to reconsideration in dialectical exchange.
4
Observe the resolution
The debate ended with each side retracting their original opinion and agreeing on tashrik—sharing the inheritance between the grandfather and siblings.
This is a concrete example of ijtihad and consensus (ijma) through debate.
5
Reflect on the dialectical method
Consider how the Sahaba used argumentation, counter-argument, and mutual concession to reach a unified ruling. This method is foundational in Islamic jurisprudence.
Compare this to modern notions of scholarly dialogue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابن عباس وابن الزبير وابن عمر وحذيفة بن اليمان ومعاذ بن جبل وأبي بن كعب وعائشة وغيرهم، وخالفه في ذلك علي وزيد بن ثابت وابن مسعود، فقالوا : لا يحجبهم بل يتقاسمون الميراث، فلم يجعلوه بمنزلة الأب، وتسميته في القرآن بالأب كانت بطريق المجاز، والرواية عن عمر ،مختلفة وإن كان المشهور عنه أنه يوافق أبا بكر في رأيه، ولقد أثر عنه أنه كان يقول لزيد وعلي : لولا رأيكما لاجتمع رأيي ورأي أبي بكر في الجد كيف يكون ابنا لي ولا أكون أباه؟! وروي أنه كان رأيه مع أبي بكر أولا ، وكان زيد بن ثابت يرى عكس رأيهما ، فلما تحاورا انتهت المحاورة برجوع كل منهما عن رأيه واتفقا على التشريك بينهما في الإرث
Ibn Abbas, Ibn al-Zubayr, Ibn Umar, Hudhayfa ibn al-Yaman, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, Aisha, and others; and opposed them in that Ali, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Ibn Mas'ud, who said: He does not exclude them, but they share the inheritance. They did not consider him in the position of the father, and his being called 'father' in the Qur'an was metaphorical. The narration from Umar is conflicting, although the well-known from him is that he agreed with Abu Bakr in his opinion. It is reported that he used to say to Zayd and Ali: 'Were it not for your opinions, my opinion and Abu Bakr's would have agreed concerning the grandfather: how can he be a son to me while I am not a father to him?' It is narrated that his opinion was with Abu Bakr first, and Zayd ibn Thabit held the opposite of their opinion. When they debated, the debate ended with each of them retracting his opinion and they agreed on sharing the inheritance between them.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents an example of ikhtilaf among the Sahaba regarding the inheritance of the grandfather (al-jadd). The author lists two groups: a majority including Ibn Abbas, Ibn Umar, Aisha, etc., who held that the grandfather excludes siblings from inheritance (like the father), and a minority including Ali, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Ibn Mas'ud, who held that the grandfather does not exclude siblings but shares the inheritance. The text notes that the term 'father' in the Qur'an when applied to the grandfather is metaphorical. The debate led to a resolution: Umar initially agreed with Abu Bakr, but after discussion with Zayd and Ali, all three retracted their original opinions and agreed on the principle of sharing (tashrik). This shows how ijtihad and dialogue can resolve differences.

التشريك
الحجب

A) They remained in disagreement.

Key Takeaway
Dialectical debate among the Sahaba, with mutual retraction, led to the agreed principle of tashrik in grandfather's inheritance.

Resolving Apparent Conflict between Texts: The Case of the Pregnant Widow's Iddah
Author illustrates ikhtilaf due to conflicting apparent meanings of Qur'anic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a case of apparent textual conflict (ta'arud) by identifying the conflicting texts, the positions of the Sahaba, and the principles used to resolve the conflict.

Core Idea
When texts conflict, scholars choose between combining (jama') and preferring (tarjih) based on strength.

In usul al-fiqh, one source of ikhtilaf is the apparent conflict between the literal meanings of two texts. This lesson presents a famous example from the Sahaba regarding the iddah of a pregnant widow. By examining the positions of Ali, Umar, and Ibn Mas'ud, you will learn how mujtahids navigate apparent contradictions by applying different interpretive principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting texts
Locate the two Qur'anic verses: al-Baqara 2:234 (four months ten days for widows) and al-Talaq 65:4 (iddah ends with delivery for pregnant women). Note that both verses are apparently general in their application.
The first verse does not mention pregnancy; the second does not mention widowhood.
2
Understand the problem
For a pregnant widow, the two verses give different end points. The question is which rule takes precedence: the general waiting period for widows or the specific waiting period for pregnant women?
3
Examine Ali's solution
Ali combined both verses, requiring the woman to wait the longer period (ab'adal-ajalayn) until both conditions are satisfied (delivery and four months ten days). This uses the principle of jama' (reconciliation).
Ali did not see a true contradiction but rather two conditions that both must be met.
4
Examine Umar and Ibn Mas'ud's solution
They gave precedence to the later-revealed verse (al-Talaq 65:4), so the iddah ends with delivery. This uses the principle of tarjih based on chronological order (naskh or preference).
They argued that the later verse abrogates or specifies the general ruling for widows.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Both approaches are valid ijtihad. The disagreement is not over the authenticity of the texts but over the method of resolving the apparent conflict. This illustrates how ta'arud is addressed in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما بسبب تعارض ظواهر النصوص ؛ مثل اختلافهم في عدة الحامل المتوفى عنها زوجها، فقال علي رضي الله عنه : تعتد بأبعد الأجلين جمعا بين الآيتين : وَالَّذِينَ يُتَوَفَّوْنَ مِنكُمْ وَيَذَرُونَ أَزْوَجًا يَتَرَبَّصْنَ بِأَنفُسِهِنَّ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ وَعَشْرًا ...) و﴿ وَأَوَلَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حَمَلَهُنَّ .... ، وقال عمر وابن مسعود : تعتد بوضع الحمل، عملا بالآية الأخيرة لتأخرها في النزول
And sometimes due to the conflict of the apparent meanings of texts, such as their disagreement concerning the iddah of a pregnant woman whose husband dies. Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, said: She observes the longer of the two periods, combining between the two verses: 'And those who are taken in death among you and leave behind wives, they shall wait for four months and ten days...' and 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their burden...' And Umar and Ibn Mas'ud said: She observes the iddah by giving birth, acting upon the latter verse because it was revealed later.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage provides another example of ikhtilaf among the Sahaba, this time arising from the apparent conflict (ta'arud) between two Qur'anic verses. The first verse (al-Baqara 2:234) specifies an iddah of four months and ten days for widows without mentioning pregnancy. The second verse (al-Talaq 65:4) specifies that the iddah of pregnant women ends with delivery. For a pregnant widow, which applies? Ali adopted a combining approach: she must wait for the longer of the two periods (ab'adal-ajalayn). Umar and Ibn Mas'ud gave precedence to the latter verse because it was revealed later, thus she ends her iddah upon delivery. Both opinions are based on ijtihad in reconciling the texts.

Key Terms
عدة
تعارض ظواهر النصوص
أبعد الأجلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the basis for Umar and Ibn Mas'ud's opinion that a pregnant widow's iddah ends with delivery?
A) They considered the first verse abrogated.
B) They followed the later-revealed verse concerning pregnant women.
C) They relied on a hadith.
D) They preferred analogy over the text.

Key Takeaway
Apparent conflict between texts does not entail contradiction; scholars use principles of reconciliation or preference to determine the operative ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify two other cases of apparent textual conflict in fiqh (e.g., verses on drinking wine vs. prohibition, or hadith on touching wudu). Apply the method of combining (jama') and preference (tarjih) to each, and state which approach the majority of jurists took.
?

How Varying Hadith Access Shapes Juristic Ijtihad
Author explains how varying access to hadith led to differing ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how disparities in prophetic knowledge among the Sahaba led to differing rulings and evaluate the logical basis for such ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Disparity in direct hearing of hadith is a primary cause of legitimate ijtihad differences.

This passage from a work on usul al-fiqh explains that the Companions often differed because they had not all heard the same hadiths. The author provides several historical examples showing how a ruling known to one Companion was unknown to another, leading to different ijtihad. Understanding this helps us appreciate that juristic disagreement can arise from legitimate epistemological limitations, not from error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause of ikhtilaf
The author explicitly states: 'their disparity in hearing from the Messenger of Allah'. This is the primary cause discussed.
2
Extract the specific knowledge disparity in each example
For each example, note what hadith knowledge one Companion had and another lacked. List: isti'dhan, grandmother inheritance, jizya from Magians, Fatima bint Qais report, raml in tawaf.
In the raml example, the disparity is not in hearing but in interpretation.
3
Classify each example into two types
Type 1: hadith not heard at all (isti'dhan, grandmother, jizya). Type 2: hadith heard but doubted or interpreted differently (Fatima's report, raml).
4
Analyse the logical consequence of discovering the hadith
If the missing hadith is later confirmed, does it automatically invalidate the earlier ijtihad? The passage implies the earlier ijtihad was valid based on available evidence.
This is a key principle in usul al-fiqh: ijtihad can be valid even if it later turns out to contradict a text that was unknown.
5
Reflect on the epistemological principle
A ruling derived from limited evidence is not necessarily wrong; it is a legitimate ijtihad given the knowledge available. This frames ikhtilaf as a natural consequence of varying data.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها تفاوتهم في السماع من رسول الله الله والتحري في الأخذ بالسنة والاجتهاد في فهمها. فربما يكون الصحابي قد سمع حكمًا أو فتوى من الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ولم يسمع الآخر ذلك الحديث، فيجتهد برأيه، وقد يوافق اجتهاده الحديث، وقد يخالفه، فكان حكم الاستئذان عند أبي موسى وجهله عمر ، وكان حكم الجدة عند المغيرة ومحمد بن مسلمة وجهله أبو بكر وعمر، وكان حكم أخذ الجزية من المجوس عند عبد الرحمن بن عوف وجهله جمهور الصحابة، وربما يبلغه الحكم، أو الحديث، ولكنه يقع في نفسه أن راوي الخبر قد وهم، كفعل عمر في خبر فاطمة بنت قيس، أو أن يرى الصحابة الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم فعل فعلا فيحمله بعضهم على القربة، ويحمله بعضهم على أنه كان على وجه الاتفاق أو لسبب زال فلا يكون مطلوبًا لأمته ؛ كالرمل في الطواف، فذهب ابن عباس إلى أن الرسول الله لفعله السبب، وهو قول المشركين
And among them is their disparity in hearing from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and in exertion in taking the sunnah and ijtihad in understanding it. Perhaps a companion may have heard a ruling or a fatwa from the Prophet and another did not hear that hadith, so he exercises his own opinion, and his ijtihad may agree with the hadith or may contradict it. Thus there was the ruling on asking permission (al-isti'dhan) with Abu Musa, and Umar was ignorant of it; and the ruling on the grandmother with al-Mughira and Muhammad ibn Maslama, and Abu Bakr and Umar were ignorant of it; and the ruling on taking jizya from the Magians with Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, and the majority of the Companions were ignorant of it. And sometimes the ruling or hadith reaches him, but he suspects that the narrator may have made a mistake, as did Umar regarding the report of Fatima bint Qays. Or the Companions may see the Prophet perform an action, and some interpret it as an act of worship, while others interpret it as something done coincidentally or for a reason that no longer exists, so it is not required for his community, such as the raml (quick walking) in tawaf. Ibn Abbas held that the Prophet did it because of a reason, which was the saying of the polytheists.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage discusses a major cause of ikhtilaf among the Sahaba: variation in their direct hearing of hadith from the Prophet. The author lists several examples. First, the ruling on asking permission (isti'dhan): Abu Musa knew a hadith about three requests for permission, while Umar did not, leading Umar to challenge Abu Musa until other witnesses confirmed it. Second, the inheritance of the grandmother: al-Mughira and Muhammad ibn Maslama knew a hadith granting the grandmother one-sixth, while Abu Bakr and Umar were initially unaware. Third, the ruling on jizya from the Magians (Zoroastrians): Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf knew the Prophet took jizya from Magians of Hajar, while the majority of companions did not know that. Fourth, Umar doubted the report of Fatima bint Qays that the Prophet did not give her housing or maintenance after triple divorce, suspecting the narrator may have erred. Fifth, the example of raml (quick walking) in tawaf: some companions understood it as a recommended act of worship, while others, like Ibn Abbas, understood it was done only to show strength to the polytheists, so the ruling is no longer applicable. These examples illustrate that varying knowledge of hadith and different interpretations of Prophet actions led to differences in rulings.

Key Terms
السماع
الاجتهاد
الرمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He had not heard the hadith about raml.
B) He thought the Prophet did it for a reason that no longer exists.
C) He considered raml to be abrogated.
D) He believed raml was only for the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement among Sahaba often stemmed from unequal access to prophetic knowledge, not from error, and their ijtihad remains valid within its epistemological context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Evaluate who wins given the available evidence.

The Methodological Sources of Islamic Law: A Parsed Inventory
Author lists the agreed-upon methodological sources of Islamic jurisprudence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize each of the thirteen sources of Islamic law enumerated in the passage, distinguishing primary from secondary sources.

Core Idea
The sources of Islamic law are a structured toolkit; each has a distinct role and evidentiary weight.

This passage from a classical usul al-fiqh text presents a systematic inventory of the sources from which rulings are derived. It begins with textual methods embedded in the Sunnah—mafhūm al-mukhālafa, mafhūm al-muwāfaqa, and tanbīh al-‘illa—then lists consensus, analogy, and other principles recognized by various schools. Your task is to parse this list methodically, understanding the function of each source and how schools differ in their acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three textual methods under the Sunnah
The passage starts with mafhūm al-mukhālafa (concept of opposition), mafhūm al-muwāfaqa (concept of agreement), and tanbīh al-‘illa (indication of the cause). These are ways the Sunnah can indicate rulings beyond its literal wording.
These are often called 'dalalat al-alfaz' or textual implications.
2
Analyze the Qur’anic example for tanbīh al-‘illa
The verse 'فإنه رجس أو فسقا' (5:3) is cited. The mention of 'رجس' (impurity) and 'فسق' (transgression) points to the cause (illa) for prohibition – here, impurity or unlawfulness.
This shows how a specific textual clue reveals the underlying rationale.
3
Count the 'five principles' and their doubling
The author says 'وهذه أصول خمسة ومن مثلها، فتكون عشرة'. Understand that the five likely refer to the primary sources (Qur’an, Sunnah, Ijma’, Qiyas, and perhaps the three methods counted as one?), while 'their like' means the secondary sources. The exact counting is debated but the point is that sources are paired.
Focus on the listing, not the arithmetic.
4
List the remaining sources from Ijma’ to shar‘ man qablanā
Write down each source in order: Ijma’, Qiyas, ‘amal ahl al-madina, Istihsan, sadd al-dhara’i‘, masalih mursala, qawl al-sahabi, murā‘āt al-khilāf, istishab, shar‘ man qablanā. For each, recall its definition from the key terms.
These are secondary sources, varying in acceptance among schools.
5
Categorize as primary (agreed-upon) vs secondary (disputed)
Primary sources (Qur’an, Sunnah, Ijma’, Qiyas) are universally accepted. Secondary sources like istihsan (Hanafi), ‘amal ahl al-madina (Maliki), etc., are accepted only by certain schools. Note which sources are more widely agreed upon.
This categorization helps in evaluating the strength of rulings derived from each source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السنة وهو مفهوم المخالفة، ثم بمفهومه (وهو) مفهوم الموافقة، ثم بتنبيهه : وهو التنبيه على العلة، كقوله تعالى : ﴿فإنه رجس أو فسقا﴾، وهذه أصول خمسة ومن مثلها، فتكون عشرة، ثم الإجماع، ثم القياس، ثم عمل أهل المدينة، ثم الاستحسان، ثم الحكم بسد الذرائع، ثم المصالح المرسلة، ثم قول الصحابي إن سنده وكان من الأعلام، ثم مراعاة الخلاف إذا قوي دليل المخالف، ثم الاستصحاب، ثم شرع من قبلنا.
The Sunnah: it is the concept of opposition, then by its concept (it is) the concept of agreement, then by its indication: it is the indication of the cause, like His statement: 'Indeed it is an abomination or transgression.' And these are five principles, and from their like they become ten. Then Ijma', then Qiyas, then the practice of the people of Medina, then Istihsan, then the ruling by blocking the means, then the public interests (masalih mursala), then the saying of a Companion if his chain is authentic and he was among the prominent scholars, then consideration of disagreement when the evidence of the opponent is strong, then Istishab, then the law of those before us.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage enumerates the primary and secondary sources of Islamic law recognized by various schools. Starting with the concept of opposition (mafhūm al-mukhālafa) and concept of agreement (mafhūm al-muwāfaqa), which are methods of deriving rulings from textual indications. Then it mentions the indication of the cause (tanbīh al-‘illa), citing a Quranic verse as an example. It notes that these are five principles, and from their like they become ten. The list proceeds to mention Ijma’ (consensus), Qiyas (analogy), the practice of the people of Medina (as favored by Malik), Istihsan (juristic preference), blocking the means (sadd al-dharā’i‘), public interests (masāliḥ mursala), the saying of a Companion (if his chain is sound), consideration of disagreement (murā‘āt al-khilāf) when the opponent’s evidence is strong, Istishāb (presumption of continuity), and the law of those before us (shar‘ man qablanā). Each of these is a recognized source in usul al-fiqh, though schools differ in their acceptance and prioritization.

Key Terms

مفهوم الموافقة
التنبيه على العلة
الإجماع
القياس
عمل أهل المدينة

سد الذرائع
المصالح المرسلة
قول الصحابي
مراعاة الخلاف
الاستصحاب
شرع من قبلنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) المصالح المرسلة
D) القياس الفاسد

Key Takeaway
This passage provides a structured inventory of the methodological tools a faqih uses to derive Islamic rulings, distinguishing between primary sources (like Ijma’ and Qiyas) and secondary ones (like istihsan and istishab).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ahmad ibn Hanbal's Hierarchy of Sources
Author outlines Ahmad's methodological hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the sources in order of priority in Ahmad ibn Hanbal's madhhab and explain the criteria for moving from one source to the next.

Core Idea
Texts first, then Companions, then weak hadith, then analogy.

Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal developed a clear hierarchy of legal sources, heavily leaning on hadith. This lesson outlines that hierarchy, showing how he prioritizes each source and the conditions for moving to the next.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source
The explicit texts of Quran and Sunnah are the first resort; when found, all else ignored.
2
Move to Companion opinions
If no text, turn to fatwas of Companions; a Companion's saying with no opposition is binding.
3
Handle disagreement among Companions
Choose the opinion closest to Quran and Sunnah; if unclear, report the disagreement without deciding.
4
Accept mursal and weak hadith
If no text or Companion saying, accept mursal and weak hadith unless contradicted by a stronger source; they take precedence over analogy.
5
Resort to analogy and blocking means
Only when all earlier sources are absent is analogy used, as a proof of necessity; also adopt sadd al-dhara'i'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإمام أحمد بن حنبل رحمه الله فقواعد مذهبه شديدة القرب من قواعد مذهب الإمام الشافعي التي تقدم ذكرها . فهو يأخذ بالنصوص من القرآن والسنة، فإذا وجدها لم يلتفت إلى سواها، ولا يقدم على الحديث الصحيح المرفوع شيئًا من عمل أهل المدينة أو الرأي أو القياس أو قول الصحابي أو الإجماع القائم على عدم العلم بالمخالف. فإن لم يجد في المسالة نصا انتقل إلى فتوى الصحابة، فإذا وجد قولا لصحابي لا يعلم له من الصحابة لم يتعده إلى غيره ولم يقدم عليه عملا ولا رأيا ولا قياسًا فإذا اختلف الصحابة تخير من أقوالهم أقربها إلى الكتاب والسنة ولم يخرج عن أقوالهم، فإن لم يتضح له الأقرب إلى الكتاب أو السنة حكى الخلاف ولم يجزم بقول منها . . . كما يأخذ بالحديث المرسل والضعيف إذا لم يجد أثرًا يدفعه أو قول صحابي أو إجماعًا يخالفه ويقدمه على القياس . . . والقياس عنده دليل ضرورة يلجأ إليها حين لا يجد واحدًا من الأدلة المتقدمة . . . ويأخذ أيضًا بمبدأ سد الذرائع.
As for Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, may Allah have mercy on him, the principles of his school are very close to the principles of Imam al-Shafi'i mentioned earlier. He takes from the texts of the Quran and Sunnah. When he finds them, he does not pay attention to anything else. He does not prefer the practice of the people of Medina, or opinion, or analogy, or the saying of a Companion, or Ijma' based on lack of knowledge of opposition over a sound marfu' hadith. If he does not find a text on an issue, he turns to the fatwa of the Companions. When he finds a saying of a Companion for which he knows no opposition from other Companions, he does not go beyond it and does not give precedence to practice, opinion, or analogy over it. If the Companions differ, he chooses from their sayings the one closest to the Book and Sunnah and does not depart from their sayings. If the closest to the Book or Sunnah is not clear to him, he narrates the disagreement without deciding on a saying... He also takes the mursal and weak hadith when he finds no effect to rebut it, or a saying of a Companion, or an Ijma' that opposes it, and gives it precedence over analogy... Analogy for him is a proof of necessity, resorted to when he does not find any of the aforementioned evidences... He also takes the principle of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i').
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the methodological principles of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal. His school is very similar to Shafi'i's, but with a few distinctive emphases. Ahmad prioritizes the explicit texts (nusus) of Quran and Sunnah absolutely; if a clear text exists, no other source is considered. He does not give precedence to any other source over an authentic marfu' hadith. When no text is found, he turns to the fatwas of the Companions. A Companion's saying with no known opposition is binding and cannot be overridden by practice, opinion, or analogy. If Companions differ, he selects the closest to the Book and Sunnah, without leaving their sayings. If the closest is unclear, he merely reports the disagreement without taking a position. Ahmad also accepts mursal and weak hadith when there is no contradicting evidence from a Companion or Ijma', and he gives them precedence over analogy. Analogy is only a last resort, a proof of necessity used when none of the earlier evidences are available. He also adopts the principle of blocking the means (sadd al-dhara'i'). This shows Ahmad's strong hadith orientation, even accepting weak hadith over analogy.

Key Terms
النصوص
الحديث الصحيح المرفوع
فتوى الصحابة
الحديث المرسل
الحديث الضعيف
القياس
سد الذرائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ahmad ibn Hanbal, which source is consulted after finding no text in Quran or Sunnah?
A) Analogy
B) Weak hadith
C) Fatwa of the Companions
D) Practice of Medina

Key Takeaway
Imam Ahmad's methodology prioritizes textual evidence, then Companion opinions, then weaker hadith, and finally analogy as a last resort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a hypothetical scenario: a new financial transaction is presented. A weak hadith exists about a similar transaction, but no text or Companion saying. Should the Hanbali jurist use the weak hadith or analogy? Explain your reasoning based on Ahmad's hierarchy.

Construct a simple diagram showing the hierarchy of sources in Ahmad's madhhab, indicating the condition for moving from one to the next.

Weak Hadith Precedes Analogy in Ahmad's Madhhab
Author details Ahmad's acceptance of weak hadith over analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions under which Ahmad ibn Hanbal accepts weak hadith and why he gives it precedence over analogy.

Core Idea
A weak textual trace is stronger than purely rational inference.

One of the distinctive features of Ahmad's usul is his willingness to use weak hadith as evidence, provided there is no stronger contradicting source. He even gives weak hadith priority over analogy. This lesson explores this rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source types
Weak hadith is from the Prophet, analogy is human reasoning.
2
Check for contradicting evidence
Ahmad accepts weak hadith only if no effect (athar) from a Companion, no Companion saying, and no Ijma' opposes it.
3
Apply the precedence rule
If weak hadith exists and no earlier source, it is used over analogy.
4
Understand rationale
Because any hadith, even if weak, is a transmission from the Prophet, which is stronger than analogy.

Analogy as a Necessity in Ahmad's Methodology
Author describes analogy as a last resort for Ahmad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when analogy becomes permissible in Ahmad ibn Hanbal's madhhab and explain why it is considered a last resort.

Core Idea
Analogy is only used when all textual and companion-based sources are exhausted.

Imam Ahmad is known for his reluctance to use analogy, limiting it to cases of absolute necessity. This lesson examines his strict conditions for employing analogy and its low position in his hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Recognize analogy's rank
Analogy is the last source after texts, Companion opinions, and weak hadith.
2
Check availability of higher sources
Before using analogy, ensure no text, no Companion fatwa, and no weak hadith (or mursal) exists.
3
Understand 'necessity' (darura)
Analogy is a proof of necessity, meaning it is only a temporary substitute.
4
Apply sadd al-dhara'i' as a related principle
Ahmad also uses blocking means, which is similar to analogy in being a rational tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ja'far al-Sadiq's Debate Against Qiyas
Narrates a historical debate critiquing the use of qiyas in religious rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ja'far al-Sadiq's three objections to qiyas and evaluate their strength in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Qiyas can contradict revealed rulings; Ja'far uses counterexamples to show its limits.

In the early development of usul al-fiqh, debates raged over the validity of qiyas (analogical reasoning). This passage records a famous exchange between Imam Abu Hanifa and Ja'far al-Sadiq, where Ja'far launches a devastating critique of qiyas using concrete examples from the Shari'a. By analyzing this debate, you will learn how dialectical reasoning (jadal) was used to test legal methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
First, identify the claim and the objection. Abu Hanifa represents the view that qiyas is a valid method for deriving rulings. Ja'far al-Sadiq opposes this, arguing that qiyas leads to error and contradicts divine rulings.
Abu Hanifa is introduced as 'the one who measures religion by his opinion.'
2
Examine the first objection: the word of shirk and iman
Ja'far asks about a word whose beginning is shirk and end is iman. Abu Hanifa cannot answer. Ja'far reveals it is 'La ilaha illa Allah' — if one says only 'La ilaha' (emptying the heart of gods), that is shirk, but adding 'illa Allah' makes it iman. This shows that changing a part changes the whole ruling, undermining simple analogical extension.
This objection targets the idea that qiyas can handle compositional changes.
3
Examine the second objection: murder vs. zina evidence
Ja'far asks which is greater in sin, murder or zina. Abu Hanifa says murder. Ja'far then points out that Allah requires only two witnesses for murder but four for zina. If murder is greater, one would expect stricter evidence, but the opposite holds. This shows that divine law does not follow the apparent logic of 'greater sin requires more proof,' so qiyas fails.
This objection uses a counterexample from the law of evidence.
4
Examine the third objection: fasting vs. prayer makeup
Ja'far asks which is greater, fasting or prayer. Abu Hanifa says prayer. Ja'far then notes that a menstruating woman makes up fasts but not prayers. If prayer is greater, one would expect it to be more obligatory to make up, yet the opposite is true. Again, this contradicts the assumption that greater acts have greater obligations.
This objection uses a well-known fiqh ruling on menstruation.
5
Determine who wins by jadal rules
Ja'far presents three counterexamples that Abu Hanifa does not refute. By the rules of jadal, the objector wins if he demonstrates a contradiction in the opponent's method that the opponent cannot explain. Here, Abu Hanifa either says 'I do not know' or remains silent. Thus Ja'far successfully undermines the claim that qiyas is a reliable method.
In jadal, the mu'tarid (objector) wins if the mudda'i (claimant) fails to rebut the objection.

وتباينت منها مواقف الفقهاء ونسوق فيما يلي واحدة من المناظرات التي شهدها هذا العصر بين رجال المدرستين وهي من صميم الجدل في أصول الفقه إذ تدور حول حجية العمل بالقياس ومدى صحة الاستدلال به في استنباط الأحكام. عن ابن شبرمة، قال : دخلت أنا وأبو حنيفة على جعفر بن محمد بن الحنفية، فسلمت عليه، وكنت له صديقا ثم أقبلت على جعفر، وقلت له : أمتع الله بك هذا رجل من أهل العراق وله فقه وعقل. فقال له :جعفر : لعله الذي يقيس الدين برأيه؟ ثم قال : أهو النعمان؟ فقال أبو حنيفة : نعم، أصلحك الله . فقال جعفر : اتق الله ولا تقس الدين برأيك فإن أول من قاس إبليس إذ أمره الله بالسجود لآدم ، فقال : قَالَ أَنَا خَيْرٌ مِّنْهُ خَلَقْنَنِي مِن نَّارٍ وَخَلَقْتَهُ مِن طِينٍ ثم قال لأبي حنيفة: أخبرني عن كلمة أولها شرك وآخرها إيمان؟ قال أبو حنيفة : لا أدري، قال جعفر : هي «لا إله إلا الله»، فلو قال: لا إله، ثم أمسك ، كان كافرًا ، فهذه كلمة أولها شرك وآخرها إيمان . ثم قال له : ويحك، أيهما أعظم عند الله ، قتل النفس التي حرم الله أو الزنا؟ قال : بل قتل النفس، فقال جعفر : إن الله قد قبل في قتل النفس شاهدين ولم يقبل في الزنا إلا أربعة، فكيف يقوم لك قياس؟ . . ثم قال : أيهما أعظم عند الله، الصوم أو الصلاة؟ قال: بل الصلاة ، قال : فما بال المرأة إذا حاضت تقضي الصيام ولا تقضي الصلاة، اتق الله يا عبد الله ولا تقس، فإنا نقف غدا نحن وأنت بين يدي الله فنقول : قال الله عز وجل وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وتقول أنت وأصحابك : قسنا ورأينا، فيفعل الله بنا وبكم ما يشاء. هذا، ومعلوم أن علم أصول الفقه قد ابتدأ تدوينه في هذه المرحلة على يد الإمام محمد بن إدريس الشافعي رحمه الله هذا التدوين الذي استصحب معه ، آثار ما قام في عصر الشافعي من جدل في مسائل الفقه والأصول، حيث نجد أنه مع بداية التأليف في أصول الفقه، ابتدأ أيضا البحث الفعلي في الجدل الأصولي كذلك، كما سنبين في الفصل الموالي.
And the positions of the jurists varied. We present below one of the debates witnessed by this era between the men of the two schools, which is from the core of dialectic in usul al-fiqh, as it revolves around the authority of acting by qiyas and the extent of the validity of reasoning by it in deriving rulings. From Ibn Shubruma, he said: I and Abu Hanifa entered upon Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Hanafiyya, so I greeted him, and I was his friend. Then I turned to Ja'far and said: May Allah benefit you, this is a man from Iraq who has fiqh and intellect. So Ja'far said to him: Perhaps it is he who measures the religion by his opinion? Then he said: Is it al-Nu'man? So Abu Hanifa said: Yes, may Allah rectify you. So Ja'far said: Fear Allah and do not measure the religion by your opinion, for the first to measure was Iblis when Allah commanded him to prostrate to Adam, and he said: 'I am better than him; You created me from fire and created him from clay.' Then he said to Abu Hanifa: Tell me about a word whose beginning is shirk and whose end is iman? Abu Hanifa said: I do not know. Ja'far said: It is 'La ilaha illa Allah.' If he said 'La ilaha' and then stopped, he would be a disbeliever. So that is a word whose beginning is shirk and whose end is iman. Then he said to him: Woe to you, which is greater in the sight of Allah, killing the soul that Allah has forbidden or zina? He said: Rather, killing the soul. Ja'far said: Indeed Allah has accepted in killing the soul two witnesses and did not accept in zina except four. So how does your qiyas stand? Then he said: Which is greater in the sight of Allah, fasting or prayer? He said: Rather, prayer. He said: Then what is the matter that when a woman menstruates she makes up the fasts but does not make up the prayers? Fear Allah, O Abdullah, and do not make qiyas. For tomorrow we and you will stand before Allah and we will say: Allah Almighty and His Messenger said, and you and your companions will say: We made qiyas and formed an opinion. And Allah will do with us and with you as He wills. This, and it is known that the science of usul al-fiqh began its codification in this stage at the hands of Imam Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. This codification brought with it the traces of what occurred in the era of al-Shafi'i of dialectic in matters of fiqh and usul. For we find that with the beginning of writing in usul al-fiqh, actual research in usuli dialectic also began, as we will explain in the following chapter.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a historical debate between Abu Hanifa and Ja'far al-Sadiq, which is used to criticize the method of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in deriving religious rulings. The passage begins by setting the context: this debate is an example of the jadal (dialectic) that occurred between the two schools regarding the authority of qiyas. The narrative starts with Ibn Shubruma introducing Abu Hanifa to Ja'far. Ja'far immediately recognizes Abu Hanifa as the one who 'measures the religion by his opinion,' referencing the use of qiyas. Ja'far then warns against qiyas, citing Iblis as the first to use analogical reasoning when he argued that fire is better than clay. This sets the tone — qiyas is linked to arrogance and error. Ja'far then poses several questions to demonstrate the inconsistency of qiyas. First, he asks about a word whose beginning is shirk and end is iman. Abu Hanifa cannot answer. Ja'far explains: 'La ilaha illa Allah' — if one says only 'La ilaha' (there is no god) and stops, that is shirk, but with 'illa Allah' it becomes iman. This shows that a change in part can change the whole ruling, undermining simple analogy. Second, Ja'far asks which is greater: murder (killing a soul) or zina. Abu Hanifa says murder is greater. Ja'far then points out that Allah requires two witnesses for murder (as in legal cases of qisas) but four witnesses for zina. If murder is greater, one would expect a higher evidentiary requirement, yet the opposite is true. This shows that divine rulings do not always follow the apparent logic of 'the greater sin requires more proof.' Third, Ja'far asks which is greater: fasting or prayer. Abu Hanifa says prayer. Ja'far then notes that a woman in menstruation must make up missed fasts but not missed prayers. If prayer is greater, one would expect it to be more mandatory to make up, but again the opposite holds. These examples demonstrate that qiyas fails because the divine law has its own logic not accessible to human reasoning. Ja'far concludes by warning that on the Day of Judgment, they will follow revelation while Abu Hanifa and his followers will rely on their own qiyas and opinions. The passage ends with a remark that usul al-fiqh began to be codified with al-Shafi'i, and that this debate exemplifies the kind of dialectical discussions that shaped the field.

الجدل

A) Because fasting is greater than prayer, yet women make up fasts.
B) Because prayer is greater than fasting, yet women are not required to make up prayers.
C) Because the ruling on menstruation has no known 'illa.
D) Because Abu Hanifa did not know the ruling on menstruation.

Key Takeaway
Ja'far al-Sadiq uses three counterexamples from the Shari'a to demonstrate that qiyas leads to contradictions, thus winning the debate by the rules of jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Write a rebuttal from Abu Hanifa's perspective defending qiyas against one of Ja'far's objections. Identify the 'illa (effective cause) that could reconcile the apparent contradiction.

The Linguistic Root of 'Aql: Restraint and Prevention
Linguistic analysis of the root 'aql

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the linguistic root of 'aql to the meaning of restraint and identify its derivations in classical Arabic lexicons.

Core Idea
'Aql in language means restraint—it holds its possessor back from blameworthy speech and action.

Before we explore the technical definitions of 'aql in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, we must first understand its linguistic foundation. Classical lexicographers like Ibn Faris and al-Khalil identified the core meaning of the root 'ayn-qaf-lam as prevention and restraint. This lesson examines their statements and shows how this meaning permeates all uses of the term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root letters
The root is 'ayn-qaf-lam (ع ق ل). Ibn Faris says it is a consistent root indicating 'habisah' (restraint) in a thing. Locate this statement in the text.
2
Examine the lexical definitions
Al-Khalil defines 'aql as the opposite of ignorance, and describes it as knowing what was unknown or refraining from action. Ibn Manzur adds 'steadiness' and 'heart'. Note how each definition includes an element of holding back or restraining.
3
Connect to the term 'aql as a restraint
The text says: 'It is called 'aql because it restrains its possessor from falling into destruction.' This shows the functional link between the linguistic root and the concept of intellect as a preventive force.

B - 'Aql in Language and Terminology. The root meaning of the word ('aql) in its linguistic origin goes back to prevention and restraint. Ibn Faris said: 'Ayn, Qaf, Lam: one consistent, regular root, which mostly indicates restraint in a thing or something close to restraint. From that comes al-'aql, which is what restrains from blameworthy speech and action.' Al-Khalil said: 'Al-'aql is the opposite of ignorance. It is said: 'aqala ya'qilu 'aqlan, if he knows what he was ignorant of before, or he refrained from what he was doing. And rajul 'aqul: if he has good understanding and abundant intellect, and ma lahu ma'qul: i.e. intellect.' (4). Ibn Manzur said: 'Al-'aql: steadiness in matters, and al-'aql: the heart, and the heart: al-'aql.' It is called 'aql because it restrains its possessor from falling into destruction, i.e., it prevents him. And it is said: al-'aql is the discernment by which man is distinguished from other animals. And it is said: so-and-so has a 'aqul heart and an inquisitive tongue, and a 'aqul heart means understanding. And 'aqala al-shay'a ya'qiluhu 'aqlan means he understood it. (1) As for terminology, the scholars have differed greatly in the definition and reality of 'aql. Perhaps the most comprehensive and precise is the saying of al-Ghazali and those who agreed with him that it cannot be defined by a single comprehensive definition because it is used homonymously in five meanings: First: its usage for the innate disposition by which man is prepared to grasp theoretical sciences and manage hidden matters. Second: its usage for some necessary matters, which appear in the essence of the discerning child concerning the possibility of possible things and the impossibility of impossible things. Third: its usage for the knowledge gained from experience, for one who has been tempered by experiences is called 'intelligent', while one who is not described so is called ignorant and foolish. Fourth: its usage for that which leads to the fruit of knowledge of consequences, by suppressing desires that call to immediate pleasures that are followed by regret; when this power is obtained, its possessor is called intelligent. Fifth: its usage for calmness and dignity, which is a praiseworthy disposition in a person in his movements and speech; so it is said: so-and-so is intelligent, i.e., he has calmness and sobriety (2). And al-Raghib al-Isfahani and others have mentioned that 'aql is used for the power prepared to receive knowledge, as it is also said for the knowledge that man acquires by that power. So every place where Allah blames the disbelievers for lack of 'aql, it refers to the second (meaning), and every place where responsibility is removed from a servant due to lack of 'aql, it refers to the first (meaning) (3).
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage examines the term 'aql (intellect/reason) in both its linguistic and technical dimensions. It begins with the linguistic root, tracing it to the meaning of prevention and restraint, as per Ibn Faris and al-Khalil. It then moves to the technical usage, where it acknowledges the difficulty of a single definition due to homonymy, and adopts al-Ghazali's fivefold classification. The five meanings are: 1) the innate capacity for theoretical knowledge and management of hidden matters; 2) necessary knowledge that appears in the discerning child, like the possibility of possible things and impossibility of impossible things; 3) experiential knowledge gained through life experience; 4) the power to know consequences and suppress immediate desires; 5) calmness and dignified composure. The passage concludes with al-Raghib al-Isfahani's distinction that 'aql can refer to both the power (capacity) and the knowledge (acquired), and clarifies that scriptural blame on disbelievers refers to the second meaning (knowledge), while removal of responsibility due to lack of 'aql refers to the first (innate capacity). This fivefold division is a key concept in usul al-fiqh and kalam, showing the multifaceted nature of human intellect.

الحبس
المنع
التمييز
القلب

Ghazali's Five Meanings of 'Aql
Ghazali's fivefold classification of 'aql

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the five meanings of 'aql as classified by al-Ghazali, and explain why a single definition is insufficient.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali showed that 'aql is homonymous—it covers five distinct concepts that cannot be unified under one definition.

Having covered the linguistic root, the passage now moves to the technical definitions. The author notes that scholars have differed greatly, and then presents al-Ghazali's solution: 'aql is used in five different senses. This lesson will unpack each of the five meanings, from innate capacity to dignified composure, and show how they relate to each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first meaning: innate capacity
The first meaning is the gharizah (innate disposition) that prepares a person to grasp theoretical sciences and manage hidden matters. This is the foundational cognitive faculty.
2
Identify the second meaning: necessary knowledge
The second meaning refers to necessary knowledge that emerges in the discerning child, such as the possibility of possible things and impossibility of impossible things. This is knowledge that is self-evident.
3
Identify the third meaning: experiential knowledge
The third meaning is the knowledge gained from life experience; one who has been tempered by experiences is called 'aql. This is practical wisdom from exposure.
4
Identify the fourth meaning: practical wisdom
The fourth meaning is the power that leads to knowledge of consequences and suppresses immediate desires for pleasure that lead to regret. This is prudence or foresight.
5
Identify the fifth meaning: dignified composure
The fifth meaning is hudu' and waqar (calmness and dignity), a praiseworthy demeanor in speech and movement. This is social intelligence or composure.

(3) المفردات في غريب القرآن للراغب الأصفهاني، ص: 346 ، الكليات لأبي البقاء العكبري،
ص : 249
B - 'Aql in Language and Terminology. The root meaning of the word ('aql) in its linguistic origin goes back to prevention and restraint. Ibn Faris said: 'Ayn, Qaf, Lam: one consistent, regular root, which mostly indicates restraint in a thing or something close to restraint. From that comes al-'aql, which is what restrains from blameworthy speech and action.' Al-Khalil said: 'Al-'aql is the opposite of ignorance. It is said: 'aqala ya'qilu 'aqlan, if he knows what he was ignorant of before, or he refrained from what he was doing. And rajul 'aqul: if he has good understanding and abundant intellect, and ma lahu ma'qul: i.e. intellect.' (4). Ibn Manzur said: 'Al-'aql: steadiness in matters, and al-'aql: the heart, and the heart: al-'aql.' It is called 'aql because it restrains its possessor from falling into destruction, i.e., it prevents him. And it is said: al-'aql is the discernment by which man is distinguished from other animals. And it is said: so-and-so has a 'aqul heart and an inquisitive tongue, and a 'aqul heart means understanding. And 'aqala al-shay'a ya'qiluhu 'aqlan means he understood it. (1) As for terminology, the scholars have differed greatly in the definition and reality of 'aql. Perhaps the most comprehensive and precise is the saying of al-Ghazali and those who agreed with him that it cannot be defined by a single comprehensive definition because it is used homonymously in five meanings: First: its usage for the innate disposition by which man is prepared to grasp theoretical sciences and manage hidden matters. Second: its usage for some necessary matters, which appear in the essence of the discerning child concerning the possibility of possible things and the impossibility of impossible things. Third: its usage for the knowledge gained from experience, for one who has been tempered by experiences is called 'intelligent', while one who is not described so is called ignorant and foolish. Fourth: its usage for that which leads to the fruit of knowledge of consequences, by suppressing desires that call to immediate pleasures that are followed by regret; when this power is obtained, its possessor is called intelligent. Fifth: its usage for calmness and dignity, which is a praiseworthy disposition in a person in his movements and speech; so it is said: so-and-so is intelligent, i.e., he has calmness and sobriety (2). And al-Raghib al-Isfahani and others have mentioned that 'aql is used for the power prepared to receive knowledge, as it is also said for the knowledge that man acquires by that power. So every place where Allah blames the disbelievers for lack of 'aql, it refers to the second (meaning), and every place where responsibility is removed from a servant due to lack of 'aql, it refers to the first (meaning) (3).
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage examines the term 'aql (intellect/reason) in both its linguistic and technical dimensions. It begins with the linguistic root, tracing it to the meaning of prevention and restraint, as per Ibn Faris and al-Khalil. It then moves to the technical usage, where it acknowledges the difficulty of a single definition due to homonymy, and adopts al-Ghazali's fivefold classification. The five meanings are: 1) the innate capacity for theoretical knowledge and management of hidden matters; 2) necessary knowledge that appears in the discerning child, like the possibility of possible things and impossibility of impossible things; 3) experiential knowledge gained through life experience; 4) the power to know consequences and suppress immediate desires; 5) calmness and dignified composure. The passage concludes with al-Raghib al-Isfahani's distinction that 'aql can refer to both the power (capacity) and the knowledge (acquired), and clarifies that scriptural blame on disbelievers refers to the second meaning (knowledge), while removal of responsibility due to lack of 'aql refers to the first (innate capacity). This fivefold division is a key concept in usul al-fiqh and kalam, showing the multifaceted nature of human intellect.

الأمور الضرورية

Al-Raghib's Distinction: 'Aql as Power vs. Knowledge
Al-Raghib's distinction between 'aql as capacity and knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Raghib al-Isfahani's distinction between 'aql as the power to acquire knowledge and 'aql as the acquired knowledge itself, and use it to interpret Qur'anic usage.

Core Idea
Al-Raghib distinguished two main senses of 'aql: as the capacity (quwwa) and as the actual knowledge ('ilm), clarifying Qur'anic verses accordingly.

The passage concludes with al-Raghib al-Isfahani's insight that 'aql can refer to either the power prepared to receive knowledge or the knowledge itself. This distinction resolves apparent contradictions in how the Qur'an uses the term. This lesson will examine this distinction and its application to scripture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of 'aql according to al-Raghib
Al-Raghib says 'aql is used for the power (quwwa) that is prepared to receive knowledge, and also for the knowledge ('ilm) acquired by that power. Note that this is a binary distinction, unlike Ghazali's five.
2
Analyze the Qur'anic usage for blame
The text states that when Allah blames disbelievers for lack of 'aql, it refers to the second sense (knowledge). Disbelievers are blamed for not having the knowledge that should lead to faith.
3
Analyze the usage for removal of responsibility
When responsibility is removed due to lack of 'aql, it refers to the first sense (capacity). For example, a child or insane person lacks the capacity, so they are not held accountable.
