Terminological Dispute on 'Daliil' for Probable Evidence
Author presents and resolves a terminological dispute on 'daliil' for probable evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions in the terminological dispute over calling probable evidence 'daliil' and explain why the author considers it merely verbal.

Core Idea
Calling probable evidence 'daliil' or 'amaarah' is a verbal dispute, not a substantive one.

In usul al-fiqh, the term 'daliil' is often used broadly for any piece of evidence, but some theologians restrict it to that which yields certain knowledge. This passage presents a dispute between jurists (who call probable evidence 'daliil') and theologians (who reserve 'daliil' for certain knowledge and use 'amaarah' for probability). The author reconciles the two sides by showing the disagreement is merely verbal, with no effect on actual reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Notice that the passage refers to two main groups: the jurists (fuqaha') and the theologians (mutakallimun). The jurists include Ibn Burhan and Ibn al-Sam'ani; the theologians include those who criticize the jurists' usage.
The passage also mentions al-Zarkashi, Ibn al-Sabbagh, and al-Khatib al-Baghdadi as commentators.
2
Extract the theologians' position
The theologians define 'daliil' strictly as that which gives certain knowledge (yaqin) of the thing indicated. For them, probable evidence (like solitary reports or analogy) is not a 'daliil' but an 'amaarah' (indicator).
This is the stricter, original sense from mantiq.
3
Extract the jurists' position
The jurists call solitary reports, analogy, and anything that leads to preponderant probability (ghalabat al-zann) a 'daliil' or 'hujja'. Their justification is that God obligated them to follow such evidences, so they are 'evidences' in the context of legal obligation.
The jurists are not denying the theologians' definition; they are extending the term contextually.
4
Find the common ground
Both groups agree that probable evidence is used for inference in legal rulings. The difference is only in the label: theologians restrict 'daliil' to certain knowledge, while jurists extend it to probable evidence. There is no disagreement on the actual legal validity or method of using such evidences.
Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi explicitly says neither party is mistaken.
5
Recognize the author's resolution
The author concludes that the dispute is verbal (lafzi), not substantive, and has no impact on reasoning. Both 'daliil' and 'amaarah' are used to arrive at legal rulings, and the responsible person (mukallaf) must follow what the evidence indicates, whether certain or probable.
This resolution is typical in usul al-fiqh: many apparent disagreements are only terminological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاول الإمام الزركشي إيجاد تبرير لمذهب القائلين بالتفريق، فقال بعد أن أورد الخلاف بين الفريقين : قيل ولعل منشأه قول بعضهم: إن الأدلة الظنية لا تحصل صفات تقتضي الظن، كما تقتضي الأدلة اليقينية العلم، وإنما يحصل الظن اتفاقا عندها، ولهذا يقولون : إن الظنيات ليس فيها ترتيب وتقديم وتأخير، وليس فيها خطأ في نفي الأمر كما يقول ذلك المصوبة. وقال ابن الصباغ : اختلف المتكلمون في إطلاق اسم الدليل على الظني وإنما قصد بهذه التسمية الفصل بين المعلوم والمظنون، فأما في أصل الوضع فلم يختلفوا في أن الجميع يسمى دليلا وضعا، وكذلك قال ابن برهان وابن السمعاني : الفقهاء لا يفرقون بينهما وفرق بينهما المتكلمون، وهو راجع إلى اللفظ دون المعنى وقد فصل الإمام الخطيب البغدادي في المسألة بما ينصف كلا الفريقين، فقال أن حكى هذا الخلاف قلت : والفقهاء يسمون أخبار الآحاد دلائل، والقياس وكل ما أدى إلى غلبة الظن سموه حجة ودليلا، والمحققون من المتكلمين وأهل النظر يعيبونهم في ذلك، ويقولون : الحجة والدليل ما أكسب المحتج والمستدل علما بالمدلول عليه وأفضى إلى يقين؛ فأما ما يفضي إليه بغلبة الظن فليس بدليل في الحقيقة، وإنما هو أمارة . ثم قال الحافظ أبو بكر الخطيب حمد الله قلت : وما غلط الفقهاء ولا المتكلمون، أما المتكلمون فقد حكوا الحقيقة في الدليل والحجة، وأما الفقهاء فسموا ما كلفوا المصير إليه بأخبار الآحاد والقياس وغيره مما لا يكسب علما وإنما يفضي إلى غلبة الظن دليلا، لأن الله تعالى أوجب عليهم الحكم بما أدى إلى غلبة الظن من طريق النظر فسموه حجة ودليلا للانقياد بحكم الشرع إلى موجبه، وقد قيل: إنما سموا ما أفضى إلى غلبة الظن دليلا وحجة في أعيان المسائل لأنه في الجملة معلوم (أعني أخبار الآحاد والقياس، وإنما يتعلق بغلبة الظن أعيان المسائل، فأما الأصل فإنه متيقن مقطوع به . فالخلاف بين الفريقين لفظي، وليس له تأثير على الاستدلال، إذ أن كلا من الدليل والأمارة يستدل بهما ويتوصل بهما إلى معرفة الأحكام الشرعية أصولا كانت أو فروعا، والمكلف مطالب باتباع ما دل عليه الدليل سواء كان ما أفضى إليه قطعا أو ظنا
Imam al-Zarkashi attempted to justify the position of those who differentiate, saying after citing the difference between the two groups: It was said that perhaps its origin is the statement of some: that probable evidences do not produce attributes that necessitate probability, as certain evidences produce knowledge, but probability occurs coincidentally by them. For this reason they say: that probabilistics have no order, priority, or posteriority, and there is no error in negating the command as those who affirm error say. Ibn al-Sabbagh said: The theologians differed over applying the name 'daliil' to the probable; they only intended by this naming to distinguish between the known and the probable. As for the origin of the term, they did not differ that all are called 'daliil' conventionally. Similarly Ibn Burhan and Ibn al-Sam'ani said: The jurists do not differentiate between the two, whereas the theologians differentiate, and this goes back to wording rather than meaning. Imam al-Khatib al-Baghdadi elaborated on the issue in a way that does justice to both groups, saying after narrating this difference: I say: The jurists call solitary reports 'daliil's, and analogy and everything that leads to preponderant probability they call 'hujja' and 'daliil'. The critical scholars among the theologians and people of theoretical reasoning criticize them for that, saying: 'Hujja' and 'daliil' are that which give the arguer and the one seeking proof knowledge of the thing indicated and lead to certainty. As for that which leads to preponderant probability, it is in reality not a 'daliil', but rather an 'amaarah'. Then al-Hafiz Abu Bakr al-Khatib (may God have mercy on him) said: I say: Neither the jurists nor the theologians are mistaken. The theologians have stated the true meaning of 'daliil' and 'hujja', while the jurists called what they are required to follow—solitary reports, analogy, and other things that do not yield knowledge but only lead to preponderant probability—a 'daliil', because God Almighty obligated them to judge by what leads to preponderant probability through theoretical reasoning, so they called it 'hujja' and 'daliil' in compliance with the ruling of the law to its implication. And it was said: They only called what leads to preponderant probability a 'daliil' and 'hujja' with respect to particular cases because in general it is known (I mean solitary reports and analogy); only particular cases involve preponderant probability, while the principle is certain and definitive. So the difference between the two groups is verbal and has no impact on reasoning, since both the 'daliil' and the 'amaarah' are used for inference and to arrive at knowledge of legal rulings, whether principles or branches, and the responsible individual is required to follow what the evidence indicates, whether it leads to certainty or probability.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute among usul scholars regarding whether probable evidence (such as solitary reports and analogy) should be called 'daliil' (evidence) or 'amaarah' (indicator). The author first reports al-Zarkashi's attempt to justify the position of those who differentiate between the two terms, quoting a view that probable evidences do not inherently produce probability but only coincidentally. Then Ibn al-Sabbagh is cited, noting that theologians differed on the application of the name 'daliil' to the probable, but that conventionally all are called 'daliil'. Ibn Burhan and Ibn al-Sam'ani state that jurists do not differentiate, while theologians do, but this is merely verbal. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi then reconciles the two sides: theologians define 'daliil' and 'hujja' strictly as that which yields certainty, while jurists apply the term to probable evidences because God obligated them to follow such evidences in practice. The author concludes that the dispute is purely verbal, without effect on actual reasoning, since both terms are used to arrive at legal rulings whether certain or probable. This passage is a dialectical analysis of a terminological controversy, showing how apparently opposed positions can be harmonized by recognizing different contexts of usage.

الدليل
الأمارة
الظن
اليقين

D) The jurists are completely mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Terminological disputes in Islamic legal theory are often verbal, not substantive, and recognizing this prevents false dichotomies.

When Reason Falls Short: The Language Origin Debate
Author argues that reason cannot decide in certain areas like the origin of languages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify matters that cannot be resolved by rational proofs and require pure revelation (al-sam' al-mahd), using the example of language origin.

Core Idea
Some questions cannot be answered by reason alone; only revelation can decide.

In this passage, al-Sam'ani and Imam al-Haramayn demonstrate that even the most rigorous rational proofs have limits. The origin of languages—whether they are divinely revealed (tawqif) or human convention (istilah)—is one such matter. This lesson will explore how Islamic scholars distinguish between domains where reason is valid and where it must defer to revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about rational proofs
Find the sentence where the author states that rational proofs cannot always be used. Look for 'لا يصح الاحتجاج بها دائما' in the Arabic.
The claim is that rational arguments are not universally applicable.
2
Locate the example of language origin
The passage gives the example of whether languages are tawqif (divinely revealed) or istilah (conventional). Identify the two possibilities mentioned.
Imam al-Haramayn permits both possibilities without preferring one.
3
Understand why reason cannot decide
The passage says 'the actual occurrence of a permissible matter is only ascertained by pure audition'. Explain that reason can only judge possibility, not actuality, in such matters.
The Arabic phrase 'لم يثبت عندنا سمع قاطع' indicates no decisive transmission exists.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
The verse 'And He taught Adam all the names' is cited. According to the passage, it is ambiguous and does not favor either view. Note the two interpretations: either God taught pre-existing languages or created them then.
This shows that even revelation can be ambiguous without decisive proof.
5
Draw the epistemological conclusion
The correct stance is suspension (tawaqquf) until decisive revelation arrives. This illustrates the principle that some questions in usul al-fiqh must be left to transmission.
This lesson shows the importance of knowing the limits of rational inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : الذي عليه الإسلاميون وغيرهم أن العقول طرق المعلومات ، قال : وأنكرت طائفة من المحدثين ذلك، وقالوا : لا يعرف شيء إلا من قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لكن هذه الأدلة العقلية لا يصح الاحتجاج بها دائما وفي كل الحالات، إذ أن هنالك حالات لا مجال للاستدلال فيها بسوى النقل أو السمع. قال السمعاني : العقول لا مجال لها في مقتضيات العبارات . ذلك قول إمام الحرمين في تجويز ثبوت اللغات توقيفا أو اصطلاحًا، دون ترجیح لأحدهما : فإن قيل : فإذا أثبتم الجواز في الوجهين عموما، فما الذي اتفق عندكم وقوعه؟ قلنا : ليس هذا الآن مما يتطرق إليه بمسالك العقول، فإن وقوع الجائز لا يستدرك إلا بالسمع المحض، ولم يثبت عندنا سمع قاطع فيما كان من ذلك، وليس في قوله سبحانه وتعالى: ﴿وَعَلَمَ ءَادَمَ الأسماء كلها﴾ دليل على أحد الجائزين، فإنه لا يمتنع أن اللغات لم يكن يعلمها فعلمه الله تعالى إياها، ولا يمتنع أن الله تعالى أثبتها ابتداء وعلمه إياها .
And Master Abu Ishaq said: The position of the Islamists and others is that intellects are the ways to knowledge. He said: A group of the Hadith scholars denied that, and they said: Nothing is known except from the saying of the Prophet (PBUH). But these rational proofs cannot be argued with always and in every situation, because there are situations where there is no room for inference except through transmission or audition. Al-Sam'ani said: Intellects have no scope in the implications of expressions. That is the statement of Imam al-Haramayn in permitting the establishment of languages by divine decree or by convention, without preferring one over the other. For if it is said: If you have established the permissibility of both aspects generally, then what do you agree actually occurred? We say: This is not something that can be reached by the paths of intellects, for the actual occurrence of a permissible matter is only ascertained by pure audition, and there has not been established for us a decisive audition in that matter. And in His saying, exalted: 'And He taught Adam all the names,' there is no proof for either of the two permissible possibilities, for it is not impossible that the languages were not known to him and then Allah taught them to him, nor is it impossible that Allah established them from the beginning and taught them to him.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage addresses the epistemological limits of rational proofs. The author quotes Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini stating that while most scholars accept reason as a path to knowledge, a group of Hadith scholars reject it entirely. However, the main point is that rational proofs are not valid in all cases. Some matters, such as the origin of languages (whether they are divinely revealed 'tawqif' or conventional 'istilah'), cannot be settled by reason. Al-Sam'ani is cited saying that reason has no role in 'implications of expressions' (muqtadayat al-'ibarat). The author then gives the example of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) who refrains from deciding whether languages are tawqifi or istilahi, because only pure transmission (sam') can determine actual occurrence. The Quranic verse about teaching Adam the names is ambiguous; it could mean God taught him pre-existing languages or created them at that moment. Reason alone cannot resolve this, so the conclusion is suspended. This lesson highlights the principle that in usul al-fiqh, some questions must be left to revelation.

مقتضيات العبارات

Types of Inqita' in Jadal: Excusable vs. Inexcusable Breaks
Author explains types of inqita' in jadal and quotes al-Juwayni.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish excusable interruptions from procedural disconnections in jadal and identify when a debater is considered disconnected according to al-Juwayni.

Core Idea
In jadal, not every break is a disconnection; only backward moves and false claims count as inqita'.

The rules of jadal impose a strict hierarchy of objections, but not every break in a debate is considered a disconnection. Sometimes a debater may seem to interrupt the flow, yet the break is excused—for example, when moving between examples or abandoning an illusory hint. However, moving backward in the objection hierarchy (e.g., from naqd to man') or making an unfounded claim about the type of objection is a genuine disconnection. This lesson, using the commentary of al-Juwayni, will help you identify both excusable and inexcusable breaks in a dialectical sequence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hierarchy of objections
Identify the different levels of objections mentioned: naqd (refutation), man' (prevention), muʿāraḍa (counterargument), farq (distinction), qawl bi al-ʿilla (statement of cause), etc. Recognize that naqd is a higher-order objection than man'.
The hierarchy matters because moving from a higher to a lower rank is problematic.
2
Identify excusable breaks
The passage lists situations that are not considered disconnection: moving between examples, abandoning an illusory tarjīḥ (like a mirage), and transitioning from evidence to something similar. These are excused because the debater is not failing to complete the argument but rather clarifying or correcting a perception.
The key is whether the break indicates an inability to complete the objection or a legitimate shift in strategy.
3
Identify inexcusable backward moves
Al-Juwayni states that moving from a higher degree to a lower one (e.g., from naqd to man' or from muʿāraḍa to naqd) is a disconnection. This signals an inability to sustain the higher-level objection.
Remember the sequence: naqd > muʿāraḍa > man'? The text implies a fixed order; backward moves are signs of weakness.
4
Identify false claims of objection types
If a debater claims to be using a specific objection (e.g., man', naqd, muʿāraḍa) but the argument does not actually meet the criteria for that type, he is also considered disconnected. The claim must match the reality.
This prevents debaters from bluffing or mislabeling their objections to avoid scrutiny.
5
Apply al-Juwayni's examples
Test scenarios: e.g., Debater A uses naqd, then switches to man'. That is a backward move and constitutes inqita'. Or Debater B claims a muʿāraḍa but it is actually a man'—false claim, so disconnected.
Practice with the examples in the passage to internalize the rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأكثر. ولأن انقطاعه عن تتميمه انقطاع عن بيان تعلقه بموضع الدلالة، فلا يبين في نفسه دلالة، وذلك عجز عن جعل الترجيح دلالة، وذلك ليس بانقطاع. ولأنه لتقريب الأمر فكان كالأمثلة والانتقال من مثال إلى مثال ليس بانقطاع. ولأن الانتقال إليه من الدليل لم يكن انقطاعا، فلأن لا يكون إلى مثله انقطاعا أولى. ولأن الترجيح تلويح وقد يلوح ما لا حقيقة له فإذا تركه علم أنه إنما تركه لأنه لاح ما لا حقيقة له فيعذر كمن يظن السراب ماء فيعذر في ظنه ذلك.

And most. And because his disconnection from completing it is a disconnection from clarifying its connection to the place of indication, so it does not itself show indication, and that is an inability to make preference an indication, and that is not a disconnection. And because it is for approximating the matter, it is like examples, and moving from example to example is not a disconnection. And because the transition to it from the evidence was not a disconnection, then it is more apt that a transition to something similar is not a disconnection. Al-Juwayni said: When he moves from a degree to what is before it, he is disconnected, like moving from refutation to prevention, or from counterargument to refutation or prevention, etc. And when he claims a question or answer to be in a certain way, and it is not so, like claiming prevention, refutation, counterargument, distinction, a statement of cause, or by virtue of shared indication, inversion, or otherwise, and it is not as he claimed, he is disconnected.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the concept of 'inqita'' (disconnection) in dialectical debate (jadal). It first provides reasons why certain actions are not considered disconnection, then defines a specific type of disconnection: moving from one objection to another improperly, and finally quotes al-Juwayni to clarify when a debater is considered 'disconnected'. The text lists multiple grounds for excusing what might appear as a disconnection: if leaving a point is due to it not indicating anything real, it is like moving between examples, which is allowed; if a hint (tarjih) is illusory, abandoning it is excusable. Then it introduces a new rule: objections have a fixed order, and moving backwards (e.g., from naqd to man') or making a claim that is not substantiated constitutes disconnection. Al-Juwayni gives examples: moving from refutation to prevention, or to counterargument, etc. Also, if a debater claims a specific type of objection (prevention, refutation, etc.) but it is not as claimed, he is disconnected. This passage emphasizes the strict procedural rules of classical jadal, where maintaining the proper sequence of objections is crucial for a coherent debate.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, only backward moves in the hierarchy of objections and false claims about the type of objection constitute genuine disconnection; other breaks are excused.

Resolving the Dispute on the Equality of Intellects
Author presents and resolves a debate on intellect equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish natural (fitri) from acquired (kasbi) intellect and explain how this distinction resolves the debate over whether intellects are equal.

Core Idea
Intellects differ in acquired knowledge, but the innate rational faculty is equal in all humans.

The passage presents a classic epistemological dispute: do human intellects vary or are they equal? The author shows that both sides have strong arguments, but the apparent contradiction dissolves once we distinguish between two types of intellect. This lesson teaches you to identify and apply this distinction in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
The passage presents a common-sense view (intellects differ) and a Mu'tazili/Ash'ari view (intellects are equal as a universal proof). Highlight the evidence each side uses: everyday speech about intelligence vs. the danger of sophistry if intellects varied.
2
Recognize the problem of sophistry
If intellects truly varied, a person could deny self-evident truths like 'one is half of two' or 'impossibility of opposites', leading to safsata (sophistry). This shows why the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya insisted on equality.
3
Examine the author's reconciliation
The author notes that both proofs are strong, so the dispute must be about different subjects. He then divides intellect into natural (fitri) and acquired (kasbi/tajribi). Natural intellect is equal; acquired varies.
4
Apply the distinction to the arguments
The common-sense view refers to acquired intellect, while the Mu'tazili/Ash'ari view refers to natural intellect. Once this is clear, the two positions no longer contradict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحوها، وذلك يدل على تفاوت العقول نفسها وأجمع العقلاء على صحة قول القائل : فلان أقل عقلا من فلان أو أكمل عقلا وذلك يدل على ما ذكرناه . وحكوا عن المعتزلة والأشعرية خلاف ذلك، لأن العقل حجة عامة يرجع إليها ذلك الناس عند اختلافهم، ولو تفاوتت العقول، لما كان كذلك، وكان يلزم من السفسطة، إذ كان يتسع لبعض الناس إذا ألزم أن الواحد نصف الإثنين، أو استحالة اجتماع الضدية أن يقول : أنتم تدركون ذلك وأنا لا أدركه ولما رأينا الناس عند الانتهاء إلى هذه القضايا، علمنا استواء الحجة تقوم مطلقا على عموم الناس في العقول. واعلم أن الحجة قوية من الطرفين وهذا يقتضي أن النزاع ليس مورده واحدا، ووجه الجمع بين القولين ؛ أن العقل على ضربين : طبعي؛ وهو الذي لا يتفاوت في العقلاء، وكسبي تجريبي؛ وهو الذي يتفاوتون فيه»(1).
And the like, and that indicates the variation of intellects themselves. And the rational people agreed on the correctness of the saying of the speaker: 'So-and-so is less intelligent than so-and-so or more intelligent', and that indicates what we mentioned. And they reported from the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya the opposite of that, because intellect is a general proof that people refer to in their disagreements, and if intellects varied, it would not be so, and sophistry would follow, since it would allow some people, when it is required that one is half of two, or the impossibility of the conjunction of opposites, to say: 'You perceive that and I do not perceive it.' And when we see people upon reaching these propositions, we know the equality of the proof that holds absolutely for all people in intellects. And know that the proof is strong from both sides, and this necessitates that the dispute is not about one subject. And the way to reconcile the two statements is that intellect is of two types: natural intellect, which does not vary among rational people, and acquired experimental intellect, which they differ in.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage discusses a foundational epistemological debate: whether human intellects are equal or vary. The author first presents the common-sense view that intellects differ, citing the accepted statement 'So-and-so is more intelligent.' Then he reports the opposing view of the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya, who argue that intellect is a universal proof (hujja 'amma) that all people refer to in disputes; if intellects varied, it would lead to sophistry (safsata), where someone could deny self-evident truths like 'one is half of two' or the impossibility of opposites. The author notes that both sides have strong evidence, indicating the dispute is not about the same thing. He reconciles by dividing intellect into two types: natural (fitri) – which is equal in all rational people – and acquired (kasbi tajribi) – which varies. This resolution is a classic example of jadal (dialectical) method: identifying the source of disagreement and clarifying terms.

Historical Roots of the Haqiqah/Majaz Debate
Author traces the debate on haqiqah and majaz to the Mu'tazilite position on grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the historical trigger that led to the haqiqah/majaz debate in usul al-fiqh and explain why a seemingly theoretical issue became central.

Core Idea
The haqiqah/majaz debate was born from a political-theological conflict, not pure theory.

Have you ever wondered why usul scholars spend so much energy on a topic like haqiqah and majaz? On the surface, it seems purely linguistic. But as Imam al-Shirazi reveals, this issue has a concrete historical origin in the early Muslim community's turmoil after the murder of Caliph Uthman. The Mu'tazilite doctrine of 'al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn' forced a debate about the literal and figurative meanings of shar'i terms, especially 'iman'. This lesson will trace that chain and show how intellectual history shapes logical-linguistic tools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the surface puzzle
Notice that the author presents the haqiqah/majaz issue as seemingly pointless: everyone agrees both forms exist in shari'a. Why then did scholars devote so much attention to it? This is the puzzle that the passage aims to solve.
2
Locate the historical event
Find the event that triggered the debate: the murder of Uthman, followed by civil conflict between Ali's supporters and the people of Sham. This political fracture gave rise to theological innovations.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite response
The Mu'tazila introduced the intermediate position (manzila bayna al-manzilatayn): grave sinners among the Companions are neither believers nor disbelievers, but rebellious sinners (fasiq). This was applied to figures like Talha and al-Zubayr.
4
Trace the linguistic objection
Opponents countered that linguistically, 'iman' means tasdiq (assent), and these Companions were musaddiqun (monotheists), so they are believers. The Mu'tazila then had to argue about the literal and figurative meanings of 'iman'—thus the haqiqah/majaz debate was born.
This shows that a theological position forced a linguistic argument, which became a major topic in usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكشف عن الأصول التاريخية لنشأة بعض المسائل والآراء فيها : هناك أحيانًا بعض المسائل نقف عليها في علم معين ولا نجد تفسيرا لانطواء هذا العلم على مثل هذه المسائل، خاصة إذا كانت مسائل يبدو أنه لا يترتب عليها عمل وليس لها فائدة عملية . من ذلك مثلا مسألة الحقيقة والمجاز في الألفاظ الشرعية)، فهذه المسألة إذا نظرنا إليها مجردة فإننا قد لا نجد مبررًا للبحث فيها لأنه لا خلاف في ورود الحقيقة والمجاز في الألفاظ الشرعية، وأن بعض المجازات اللغوية قد أصبحت حقائق شرعية، لكننا نجد الأصوليين يولون هذه المسألة عناية واضحة ويشتغلون بإيراد الآراء والأدلة في شأنها. ويبين لنا الإمام الشيرازي سر هذا الاهتمام حين بالمسألة إلى نشأتها التاريخية والظروف التي دعت إلى ظهورها، وذلك إذ يقول: هذه أول مسألة نشأت في الاعتزال، وذلك أن عثمان رضي الله عنه ، لما قتل، ظهرت البدع وكثرت الشرور، فقوم من أصحاب علي تبرؤوا منه، وقال أهل الشام : نحن نطلب دم عثمان)، وجرت بينهم من الحروب ما لا يخفى، فجاءت المعتزلة بعدهم بقليل فقالوا : ننزلهم منزلة بين المنزلتين، فلا نسميهم كفارا ولا مؤمنين، ونقول : هم فسقة، حتى أطلقوا هذا القول على عظماء الصحابة كطلحة والزبير حتى قال كبراؤهم . . . : لو) شهد عندي علي وطلحة على باقة، لم أقبل حتى يكون معهما ثالث لأن أحدهما فاسق)، فقيل لهم : (إن الإيمان في اللغة هو التصديق وهؤلاء مصدقون (موحدون)، فقالوا : (إن هذا
Uncovering the historical origins of the emergence of some issues and opinions therein: Sometimes we come across some issues in a particular science and find no explanation for why this science includes such issues, especially if they are issues that seem to have no practical consequence or benefit. For example, the issue of haqiqah and majaz in shar'i terms. If we look at this issue abstractly, we might find no justification for researching it, because there is no disagreement about the occurrence of haqiqah and majaz in shar'i terms, and that some linguistic majaz became shar'i haqiqah. But we find the usul scholars giving this issue clear attention and engaging in presenting opinions and evidences concerning it. Imam al-Shirazi explains the secret of this interest when he traces the issue to its historical origin and the circumstances that led to its emergence. He says: This is the first issue that arose in I'tizal. That is because when Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, was killed, innovations appeared and evils increased. A group from the companions of Ali disassociated themselves from him, and the people of Sham said: 'We seek Uthman's blood.' Wars occurred between them that are well known. The Mu'tazila came shortly after and said: 'We place them in a position between two positions; we do not call them disbelievers nor believers, but we say they are sinners.' They even applied this statement to the great companions like Talha and al-Zubayr, until their leaders said: 'If Ali and Talha were to testify before me regarding a bundle of herbs, I would not accept until a third is with them, because one of them is a sinner.' It was said to them: 'Faith in language is tasdiq (assent), and these are musaddiqun (those who give assent, i.e., monotheists).' They said: 'This...'
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe that explains why a seemingly theoretical issue in usul al-fiqh—the distinction between haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative) in shar'i terms—received so much attention. The author argues that when considered abstractly, the issue seems pointless because everyone agrees that both literal and figurative usage occur in religious language. The reason Muslim scholars invested so much effort in it was historical, not purely logical. Al-Shirazi traces it to the early theological conflict after the murder of Uthman. The Mu'tazila adopted a middle position regarding the grave sinners among the Companions, calling them 'sinners' rather than believers or disbelievers. Their opponents argued that such Companions were still believers because the linguistic meaning of iman is tasdiq (assent), and they did assent (tawhid). This forced the Mu'tazila to argue about the literal and figurative meanings of terms used in scripture and language. Thus, the debate on haqiqah and majaz developed as a response to a specific historical controversy. The passage illustrates that even logical and linguistic discussions can have deep roots in kalam and political history.

Key Terms
الحقيقة والمجاز
المعتزلة
المنزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because scholars disagreed on whether shari'a terms have literal or figurative meanings.
B) Because the Mu'tazila needed to argue that the term 'iman' does not linguistically apply to those they deemed sinners.
C) Because it was a purely logical problem inherited from Aristotle.
D) Because the early usul scholars had a theoretical interest in language.

Key Takeaway
The haqiqah/majaz debate in usul al-fiqh was historically triggered by the Mu'tazilite attempt to justify their intermediate position on grave sinners, forcing them to argue about the literal and figurative meanings of key shari'a terms.

Ethical Foundations of Productive Debate in Jadal
Author advocates intellectual freedom and warns against oppressing the opponent in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ethical conditions for productive dialectical debate as prescribed by classical usuliyyun and evaluate whether a given debate adheres to those principles.

Core Idea
Intellectual freedom and mutual respect are the prerequisites for truth to emerge in debate.

This passage underscores that productive jadal (dialectical debate) requires more than logical skill; it demands intellectual freedom and fair treatment of the opponent. Drawing from Ibn Hazm and Ibn 'Aqil, the author explains that oppression stifles thought and prevents the discovery of truth. In this lesson, we will extract the key ethical principles that ensure debate remains a cooperative search for truth rather than a contest of ego.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ethical condition for debate
Read the passage's emphasis on intellectual freedom. The author states that beneficial research is only based on freedom of thought and expression. Notice the quote from Ibn Hazm about valuing criticism over praise.
2
Extract Ibn 'Aqil's warning about oppression
Find where Ibn 'Aqil warns that injustice (zulm) against the opponent corrupts the debate. Note the three harmful effects: breaking the opponent's spirit, killing thought, and cutting off reliance on the process.
3
Analyze how usuliyyun cultivated equality
The passage notes that the usuliyyun practiced al-nadiyya (parity) in debates, avoiding arrogance and cooperating to reach truth. Look for evidence of mutual respect in their historical practice.
4
Apply these principles to a hypothetical debate
Consider a scenario where a debater insults the opponent's school of thought. Evaluate whether this violates intellectual freedom or equality, and what Ibn 'Aqil would predict as the consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثقته بفكره وبقابليته لأن يكون طرفا للحوار، فيتجمد عند ذلك ويفقد قدرته على الحركة الفكرية، فيتحول إلى صدى للأفكار التي يتلقاها من الآخر» (1) ويقول المثل الشائع : الحقيقة بنت البحث، ولا يكون البحث النافع إلا على قاعدة حرية التفكير والتعبير، ولله در الإمام ابن حزم حيث يقول : من حقق النظر وراض نفسه على الحقائق وإن آلمتها في أول صدمة كان اغتباطه بذم الناس إياه أشد وأعظم من اغتباطه بمدحهم إياه، لذلك كان للمجادل أن يقول كل ما يجول بخاطره في شأن ما يبحث، وتعين على مناظره أن يصغي بتفهم كل ما يُسرد أمامه على بساط البحث من غير تأفف أو ضجر، ولو كان ما يقال مخالفا لرأيه و اعتقاده، إذ طالما سمعنا كلاما خلناه في أول الأمر خطأ أو وهما أو سهوا، ولكننا بعد التريث والبحث والاستقصاء ألفيناه الصواب بعينه وأننا المخطئون» (2) فغياب الحرية الفكرية وتعدي طرف على آخر أو حجره عليه، من شأنه أن يحرم موضوع البحث من الثراء والتوسع ويدفع الباحث إلى التخلي عن الموضوع والبعد عن التأمل فيه .
لذلك يحذر الإمام ابن عقيل قائلا : كل جدل يقع فيه ظلم الخصم فإنه يختل بحسب قوة ذلك وضعفه وذلك أن ظلم الخصم يكسر من نفسه، أو يزيله عن طريقته، فينبغي أن يحترز منه، إذ كان انكسار النفس يميت الخاطر، ويقطع عن بلوغ الآخر، والزوال عن الطريق يخرج عن الاعتماد، ومن عرف من عادته ظلم الخصم فليس ينبغي أن يتهكم إلا أن يرجع إلى الإنصاف، أو يدفع إلى ذلك حال، فيحترز منه غاية الاحتراز» (3) . وقد عمل الأصوليون فيما أداروه من محاورات جدلية مع مخالفيهم أن يهيئوا الندية بينهم وبين مخالفيهم، فلم نقف في استقرائنا لمختلف المباحث الجدلية الأصولية على ما يمكن أن نسميه تطاولا على الطرف الآخر من هذا الطرف أو ، وإنما وقفنا على نماذج كثيرة من التكافؤ في الحوار، والحرص على النظر
إلى الطرف الآخر نظرة الندية والتكافؤ، ونظرة التعاون المشترك للوصول إلى الحق .
His confidence in his thought and his ability to be a party to the dialogue, then he freezes at that and loses his capacity for intellectual movement, turning into an echo of the ideas he receives from the other. And the common saying goes: Truth is the daughter of research, and beneficial research is only based on freedom of thought and expression. How excellent is what Imam Ibn Hazm says: Whoever thoroughly examines and trains himself on truths, even if they pain him at first shock, his joy at people's blame is greater and more intense than his joy at their praise. Therefore, the disputant may say whatever crosses his mind regarding the matter under discussion, and his opponent must listen attentively to everything presented before him on the discussion table without annoyance or boredom, even if what is said contradicts his opinion and belief, for how often have we heard something we initially thought was wrong or illusory or mistaken, but after deliberation, research, and investigation, we found it to be the very truth and that we were the mistaken ones. So the absence of intellectual freedom and the transgression of one party over another or restricting him would deprive the subject of research of richness and expansion and drive the researcher to abandon the subject and distance himself from reflection. Therefore, Imam Ibn 'Aqil warns, saying: Every debate in which injustice occurs against the opponent becomes corrupt according to the strength or weakness of that injustice, because injustice to the opponent breaks his spirit or removes him from his method, so one must be cautious of it, since breaking the spirit kills the thought and cuts off reaching the other, and deviation from the path removes reliance. Whoever knows from his habit the injustice of the opponent should not mock unless he returns to fairness, or a situation compels him to that, so he should be extremely cautious. And the legal theorists (usuliyyun) in their dialectical discussions with their opponents have worked to establish equality between themselves and their opponents, for we have not found in our survey of various dialectical investigations of usuliyyun anything we could call arrogance towards the other party from this side or that, but rather we have found many examples of equality in dialogue, and care to look at the other party with a view of parity and equality, and a view of mutual cooperation to reach the truth.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage discusses the essential conditions for productive dialectical debate. It begins by stressing the importance of intellectual freedom: if a disputant loses confidence in his own ability to engage, he becomes passive and merely echoes others. The author then quotes the proverb 'Truth is the daughter of research' to emphasise that fruitful inquiry requires freedom of thought and expression. A lengthy quote from Imam Ibn Hazm illustrates that a true seeker of truth should value criticism over praise, as it drives honest self-examination. The text then warns that lack of freedom and oppression of the opponent impoverishes discussion and leads to abandonment of the topic. Imam Ibn 'Aqil is cited to highlight the damaging effects of injustice in debate: it breaks the opponent's spirit, kills creative thought, and undermines reliance on the process. Finally, the author notes that the early legal theorists (usuliyyun) practiced mutual respect and equality in their debates, avoiding arrogance and striving for cooperative truth-seeking. The key takeaway is that intellectual freedom and fair treatment are foundational to successful jadal.

Adaptation to the Listener's Level in Debate
Author explains the necessity for the speaker to adapt to the listener's cognitive level.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate a debater's adaptation to the listener's level by applying the principles of intellectual convergence and proportional means of persuasion.

Core Idea
Effective persuasion requires bridging the gap between speaker's knowledge and listener's understanding.

In jadal, the goal is not simply to win but to arrive at truth through mutual understanding. This lesson explores the fourth procedural principle: the debater must adapt to the listener's cognitive level. Failure to do so leads to misunderstanding and prevents the debate from reaching any meaningful conclusion. The author emphasizes that the means of persuasion must be proportionate to the listener's capacity for conviction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Locate the author's statement that intellectual convergence is required between debate parties. Note the consequence: if views diverge without adaptation, the debate misses its mark and yields no scientific value.
2
Trace the reasoning for adaptation
Follow the author's explanation that the listener only has access to the speaker's spoken words, not the full context in the speaker's mind. The speaker must bridge this gap by revealing enough to induce conviction.
This asymmetry is a key epistemological insight.
3
Assess the proportionality condition
Examine the claim that the means of persuasion should match the means of conviction of the listener, and never be below them. Consider why this is crucial—if the speaker's arguments are too complex or too simple, persuasion fails.
This ensures the argument is accessible without being diluted.
4
Apply the gatekeeping rule
Note the warning against debating with someone who lacks expertise on the subject. Ignorance leads to stubbornness and potential disrespect. This protects the integrity of the debate and the participants.
This is a practical guideline for selecting appropriate interlocutors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
4 - تكيف المتحدث مع مستوى المستمع :
التقارب الفكري بين طرفي الجدل أمر مطلوب لنجاح العملية الجدلية، وفي حال تباعد رؤيتيهما حول المسألة، فعلى كل منهما أن يحاول التكيف مع مستوى معرفة الآخر في المسألة موضع الجدل والنقاش وإلا فإن عملية الجدل ستدور في غير موضعها الصحيح، ولن تقود إلى نتيجة ذات قيمة علمية . ذلك أن أول ما يفسد النقاش هو عدم الفهم، ولهذا يتعين على المستدل أن يأخذ بعين الاعتبار وضعية المستمع من الأمر الذي يريد محاورته فيه، ومهما تكن درجة إحاطة المستدل بالموضوع الذي يريد إثباته، شاملة، ومهما تكن درجة ثقته في صحة ما يقدم كاملة، فإن وقع ذلك على المستمع سيكون بحسب موقع هذا المستمع من موضوع النقاش ومن معارف المستدل، إذ كثيرًا ما يظن المتحدث خطأ أن المستمع يتصور الأمور كما يتصورها هو، وإذا كان المستمع لا يستطيع أن يطلع من عالم معارف المتحدث إلا على ما يرد على لسان هذا الأخير، فإن قسمًا كبيرًا من عالم المعارف هذا يبقى مجهولاً لدى المستمع، وبالتالي غير فعال على تفكيره، بينما هو لدى المتحدث جزء من كل يندرج فيه مع سائر الأجزاء الأخرى ينسجم معها عند جريان التفكير لديه ؛ ولهذا ستكون قدرة المستدل على الإقناع متناسبة مع قدرته على إطلاع المستمع على جميع ما يستدرجه إلى الاقتناع، بل إن وسائل الإقناع لدى المستدل ينبغي أن تكون متناسبة مع وسائل الاقتناع لدى المستمع، فلا تكون دونها أبدًا .
وكما ينبغي لكل من المتجادلين أن يتكيف مع المستوى الفكري للآخر، ينبغي كذلك لكل منهما أن لا يفتح باب الجدل مع غيره إذا رأى أن هذا الغير ليس متخصصا في الموضوع وليس له معرفة سابقة به، لأن جهله بالمسألة سيحمله على التعنت والتكلف لما لا يعلم وربما قاده ذلك إلى إهانة الطرف المتخصص .
4 - Adaptation of the speaker to the listener's level:
Intellectual convergence between the two parties of debate is required for the success of the dialectical process. In case of divergence of their views on the issue, each of them must try to adapt to the level of knowledge of the other on the issue under debate and discussion; otherwise, the debate will revolve in the wrong place and will not lead to a result of scientific value. That is because the first thing that spoils discussion is lack of understanding. Therefore, the reasoning party must take into consideration the situation of the listener regarding the matter he wants to discuss. Whatever the comprehensiveness of the reasoning party's knowledge of the subject he wants to prove, and whatever the completeness of his confidence in the correctness of what he presents, when it reaches the listener, it will be according to the listener's position regarding the subject of discussion and the knowledge of the reasoning party. For often the speaker mistakenly thinks that the listener conceives things as he conceives them. If the listener cannot access from the speaker's world of knowledge except what is uttered by the latter's tongue, then a large part of this world of knowledge remains unknown to the listener, and consequently ineffective on his thought, while for the speaker it is part of a whole that is integrated with other parts and consistent with them in his thinking. Hence, the reasoning party's ability to persuade will be proportional to his ability to inform the listener of everything that leads him to conviction; indeed, the means of persuasion of the reasoning party should be proportional to the means of conviction of the listener, never below them.
And just as each of the two debaters should adapt to the intellectual level of the other, so too should each of them not open the door of debate with another if he sees that the other is not specialized in the subject and has no prior knowledge of it, because his ignorance of the issue will lead him to stubbornness and affectation for what he does not know, and perhaps that will lead him to insult the specialized party.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a practical instruction for effective dialectical engagement. It presents the fourth point: adaptation of the speaker to the listener's level. The author asserts that intellectual convergence between debaters is essential; if their views diverge, each must adapt to the other's level of knowledge, otherwise the debate misses its mark and yields no scientific value. Misunderstanding is the primary spoiler of discussion. The reasoning party (mustadill) must consider the listener's perspective and realise that the listener only has access to what the speaker says, not the full context in the speaker's mind. Therefore, the speaker must bridge this gap by revealing enough to induce conviction. Moreover, the means of persuasion should match the listener's means of conviction. The passage also advises against debating with someone who lacks expertise in the subject, as ignorance leads to obstinacy and potential disrespect. Thus, this lesson emphasises empathetic communication and audience awareness in theological-legal dialectic.

Key Terms
تكيف (takayyuf): Adaptation of the speaker's discourse to the listener's intellectual level and knowledge background to ensure effective communication and persuasion.
وسائل الإقناع (wasa'il al-iqna'): The means or methods of persuasion employed by the reasoning party, which should be proportionate to the listener's capacity for conviction.
المستمع (al-mustami'): The listener or recipient of the argument, whose level of understanding and background must be considered for successful persuasion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Lack of evidence
B) Lack of understanding
C) Emotional outbursts
D) Disagreement on premises

Key Takeaway
Successful debate requires the speaker to bridge the gap between his own comprehensive knowledge and the listener's limited access, by adapting both content and means of persuasion to the listener's level.

Resolving the Dispute on Obligation with Choice (Wajib Mukhayyar)
Author analyzes the Mu'tazila claim about wajib al-jami' and takhyir and resolves it as a terminological dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Mu'tazila's intended meaning and the majority's misinterpretation of obligation with choice, and identify the verbal nature of the dispute.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's 'obligation of all' does not contradict choice; it means the set is obligatory, not each act.

In usul al-fiqh, the concept of wajib mukhayyar (obligatory with choice) appears contradictory at first glance. This passage from al-Tufi analyzes a Mu'tazila claim that seems to suggest both that all actions are obligatory and that there is choice. The author clarifies that the disagreement is only verbal, and the error stems from misunderstanding the intended meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
The Mu'tazila state 'all are obligatory with choice'. On the surface, 'obligation of all' requires doing all actions, while 'choice' allows doing any one. These seem incompatible. Locate this tension in the Arabic.
Look for the phrase 'يجب الجميع على التخيير'.
2
Clarify the Mu'tazila's intended meaning
The author explains that by 'obligation of all', the Mu'tazila mean 'it is not permissible to abandon all'. They do not mean each individual act is obligatory. This is a crucial distinction.
Find where the author says 'مرادهم بوجوب الجميع أنه لا يجوز ترك الجميع'.
3
Locate al-Qarafi's support
Al-Qarafi confirms that the obligation attaches to the set (all), but liability is cleared by doing any part. This aligns with the author's interpretation.
See the quote: 'إن الوجوب تعلق بالجميع على وجه تبرأ الذمة بفعل البعض'.
4
Determine the author's resolution
The author concludes that the disagreement is purely verbal (khilaf lafzi). The error lies either with the Mu'tazila for thinking obligation and choice cannot combine, or with the majority for misinterpreting the Mu'tazila's wording.
The author says 'الخلاف لفظي' and identifies the possible source of error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك ما ساقه الطوفي في مسألة الواجب المخير، من رأي للمعتزلة، إن قولهم : يجب الجميع على التخيير، كلام متناقض في نفسه، إذ مقتضى وجوب الجميع : أن لا يبرأ إلا بفعل الجميع ومقتضى التخيير ؛ أنه يبرأ بفعل أيها شاء، وهما لا يجتمعان، وإنما مرادهم بوجوب الجميع أنه لا يجوز ترك الجميع، وهو صحيح، لكن لا يلزم منه وجوب فعل الجميع، أو وجوب الجميع على البدل، لا على الجمع بمعنى إن لم تفعل هذا افعل هذا، وهو مذهب الجمهور. في وقال القرافي في معناه : إن الوجوب تعلق بالجميع على وجه تبرأ الذمة بفعل البعض، وهو معنى ما قلناه، وتبين بذلك أن الخلاف لفظي، وأقول : إن الغلط المسألة، إما من المعتزلة، حيث ظنوا أن الوجوب مع التخيير لا يجتمعان، أو من الجمهور على المعتزلة بأن رأوا لهم عبارة موهمة أو بعيدة الغور، فظنوا أنهم أرادوا وجوب الجميع، كما وهموا عليهم في تلخيص مسألة تحسين العقل وتقبيحه، وغيرها من المسائل التي توجد في كتب المعتزلة، على خلاف المنقول عنهم فيها
That is what al-Tufi mentioned regarding the issue of the obligatory with choice, from the opinion of the Mu'tazila: their statement 'all are obligatory with choice' is a self-contradictory statement, because the requirement of 'obligation of all' is that one is not cleared except by doing all, and the requirement of 'choice' is that one is cleared by doing any of them, and they cannot be combined. However, what they mean by 'obligation of all' is that it is not permissible to abandon all, which is correct, but it does not necessitate the obligation of doing all, or the obligation of all on substitution, not on conjunction, meaning: if you do not do this, then do that, and that is the position of the majority. Then al-Qarafi said regarding its meaning: the obligation is attached to all in such a way that one's liability is cleared by doing some, which is the meaning we stated, and thereby it became clear that the disagreement is verbal. I say: the mistake in the issue is either from the Mu'tazila, where they thought that obligation and choice cannot be combined, or from the majority against the Mu'tazila, because they saw that their expression was misleading or remote in meaning, so they thought they intended obligation of all, just as they misunderstood them in the summary of the issue of the rational goodness and evil, and other issues found in the books of the Mu'tazila, contrary to what is reported about them.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical analysis of a disputed issue in usul al-fiqh: the Mu'tazila's claim that all actions are obligatory, but with a choice (takhyir). The author first states the apparent contradiction: if all are obligatory, one must do all; if choice, one may do any. These seem incompatible. However, he clarifies that the Mu'tazila's intended meaning is not that every individual action is obligatory, but that it is not permitted to abandon all of them together. This is actually the position of the majority (Jumhur). Al-Qarafi supports this interpretation, noting that the obligation attaches to the set, but the liability is discharged by doing any part. The author concludes that the dispute is merely verbal (khilaf lafzi), and that the error lies either in the Mu'tazila's misunderstanding of the compatibility of obligation and choice, or in the majority's misinterpretation of the Mu'tazila's expression. This analysis demonstrates the importance of precise terminology in Islamic dialectics.

Key Terms
الواجب المخير
وجوب الجميع
التخيير

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) There is no obligation at all.

Key Takeaway
Disagreements in usul al-fiqh are often verbal; careful analysis of intended meanings can resolve apparent contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look up a modern fatwa that discusses multiple options for an obligatory act (e.g., kaffarah for breaking an oath). Analyse whether the mufti treats the obligation as attaching to the set or to each option individually. Use the framework from this lesson to identify any potential verbal disputes.

The Principle of Patient Listening in Dialectical Debate
Author establishes the Quranic and prophetic basis for patient listening in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic and prophetic foundations for the rule of allowing each debater to fully express their view.

Core Idea
Patience in listening is a duty; it reveals hidden agreement.

This lesson explores the foundational principle that patience—allowing the other party to finish speaking—is a requirement of respect in dialectical debate. The author grounds this in Quranic commands to listen and the Prophet's example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the opening sentence. What core etiquette is being taught? The author states that patience—letting the other finish—is a requirement of respect in dialectical debate.
2
Find the Quranic evidence
Look at the Quranic verses cited. The first commands attentive listening to the Quran (الإنصات). The second commands listening to speech to follow the best. How do these support the rule?
Both verses emphasize complete reception before response.
3
Examine the Prophetic example
The author notes that the Prophet would listen to his companions until they finished before responding. This models the principle in practice.
This is drawn from well-known incidents in the Sira.
4
Understand the benefit
What practical advantage does full expression offer? It can reveal hidden agreement, turning perceived disagreement into consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامه صبر كل منهما على الآخر حتى ينهي من مقتضيات احترام كل من طرفي الجدل للطرف الآخر؛ أن يصبر كل منهما على الآخر حتى يعبر عن رأيه في المسألة المتنازع فيها تعبيرا كاملا، بحيث ينهي كلامه بنفسه وليس بأن يوقفه الآخر. وهذا من الأدب الرفيع الذي أدب الإسلام به المسلمين، فأمرهم بالإنصات للقرآن حتى يفهموا مراد الله من كلامه ومن ثم تنزل عليهم رحمة الله التي أودعها الإيمان والصلاح والهدى والرشاد والشفاء الذي تضمنها القرآن الكريم، كتابه من قال تعالى : وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْآنُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ وَأَنصِتُوا لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ (2) وقال موجها المؤمنين للاستماع إلى القول استماعا تاما ليأخذوا منه أحسنه : الَّذِينَ يَسْتَمِعُونَ الْقَوْلَ فَيَتَّبِعُونَ أَحْسَنَهُ (1) . وقد طبق الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ، ذلك ، فكان ينصت لأصحابه حتى يفرغ محدثه منهم من كلامه ثم يبدأ هو بالكلام، وذلك مستفيض ومشهور من مقابلاته وإجابته على أسئلة السائلين ونحو ذلك مما هو معروف من سيرته التي أمرنا بالاقتداء بها (2). إن سماح كل طرف للطرف الآخر بالتعبير عن رأيه وصبره عليه حتى ينهي کلامه إلى جانب أنه احترام للرأي الآخر ، فيه أيضًا فائدة للجدل، وهي وضوح الرؤية بالنسبة لكل طرف في شأن ما يراه الطرف الآخر ، إذ ربما كان الطرفان على رأي واحد في المسألة وهما يعتقدان أنهما على خلاف فيها فيكون التعبير الكامل عن الرأي طريقا إلى إظهار هذا التوافق بعد اعتقاد التخالف قال الجويني : «عليهما جميعا أن يصبر كل واحد منهما لصاحبه في نوبته، وإن كان ما يسمعه منه شبه الوسواس، لأنهما متساويان في حق المناوبة، فمن لم يصبر منهما لصاحبه، فقد قطع عليه حقه، ولأن ما يظنه السامع وسواسا ربما يكون هو موضع الالتباس والشبهة عنده فلابد من الصبر على سماعه ليصير عنده معلوم الأول والآخر، ثم يتكلم فيه بعده على حقه، وإن كان فيما يصح العلم به صنوان، كان فيما لا يفهم منه شيء بيَّن في نوبته أنه مما لا يلزمه الكلام عليه. ومتى لم يصبر له خصمه، بل داخله بالاعتراض أو الجواب في نوبته، احتمله ووعظه، فإن أصر عليه قطع مكالمته وإن حكم الجاهل من الحاضرين عليه بالانقطاع، لأنه إنما افترض عليه الكشف عن الحق والأمر بالمعروف مع القدرة، ذلك ولا سبيل إليه مع التقطيع، بل يصح مع حسن الاستماع (3) إن حرمان أحد طرفي الجدل من التعبير عن رأيه، إلى جانب أنه ظلم له، هو كذلك ظلم للعملية الجدلية نفسها، حيث نحرم الموضوع من التكامل ونحرم
His speech: Patience of each with the other until he finishes is among the requirements of respect of each party of the dialectic for the other; that each be patient with the other until he expresses his opinion in the disputed issue fully, such that he finishes his speech by himself and not by the other stopping him. This is from the refined etiquette with which Islam disciplined the Muslims, for He commanded them to listen attentively to the Qur'an until they understand God's intent from His speech and then God's mercy which He deposited in faith, righteousness, guidance, rectitude, and healing which the Noble Qur'an contains descends upon them, from His Book. He, exalted, said: 'And when the Qur'an is recited, listen to it and be silent, that you may receive mercy.' (2) And He said, directing the believers to listen to speech completely to take the best of it: 'Those who listen to speech and follow the best of it...' (1). The Messenger, may God bless him and grant him peace, applied that, for he would listen to his companions until his interlocutor from among them finished his speech, then he would begin speaking. This is widespread and well-known from his meetings, his answers to questioners, and the like, which are known from his biography that we are commanded to emulate (2). Indeed, allowing each party for the other to express his opinion and patience with him until he finishes his speech, besides being respect for the other opinion, also contains a benefit for dialectic, which is clarity of vision for each party regarding what the other party sees. For perhaps the two parties hold the same opinion on the issue while they think they are in disagreement, so full expression of the opinion is a way to reveal this agreement after belief of disagreement. Al-Juwayni said: 'Both must be patient with each other in his turn, even if what he hears from him resembles delusion, because they are equal in the right of turn-taking. Whoever does not be patient with his companion has cut off his right. And because what the listener thinks is delusion may be the place of confusion and doubt for him, so patience in listening is necessary so that its beginning and end become known to him, then he speaks about it afterwards as it deserves. And if it is among what is soundly known to be two of a kind, then if there is something not understood from it, he clarifies in his turn that it is something he does not need to speak about. And when his opponent is not patient with him, but rather interrupts him by objection or answer in his turn, he should tolerate and admonish him. If he persists, he should cut off his conversation, even if the ignorant among the attendees judges him to be cut off, because he is only obligated to uncover the truth and enjoin good with ability, and that is not possible with interruption, rather it is possible with good listening (3). Indeed, depriving one party of dialectic from expressing his opinion, besides being an injustice to him, is also an injustice to the dialectical process itself, where we deprive the subject of completeness and we deprive...'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a lesson on the etiquette (adab) of dialectical debate. It begins by stating that patience—allowing the other party to finish their speech completely—is a requirement of respect. The author roots this principle in Islamic teachings: God commands attentive listening to the Qur'an, and the Prophet modeled it by letting companions finish before responding. A key benefit is that full expression can reveal hidden agreement, turning perceived disagreement into consensus. The author then quotes al-Juwayni at length, who emphasizes that both debaters have equal right to speak in turn, and even if the speech seems like nonsense, it may actually reveal the source of confusion. Interrupting is a violation of the opponent's right. Al-Juwayni advises that if the opponent persists in interrupting, one should admonish, and if necessary, cut off the conversation, even if bystanders think it's a loss. The core principle is that dialectic aims at uncovering truth, which requires complete and patient listening. The passage concludes by noting that silencing a party harms the dialectical process by depriving it of completeness.

Key Terms
الجدل
المناوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only Quranic verse 39:18
C) Both 7:204 and 39:18, plus prophetic example
D) Only prophetic example

Key Takeaway
Patient listening is a fundamental Islamic etiquette that ensures respect and clarity in dialectical debate.

Al-Juwayni's Etiquette of Turn-Taking and Handling Interruptions
Al-Juwayni provides a detailed protocol for turn-taking and responding to interruptions in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Juwayni's guidance for maintaining respectful turn-taking in a dialectical exchange, including how to respond to interruptions.

Core Idea
Even seemingly nonsensical speech must be heard; interruptions are a violation of the opponent's right.

In this lesson, we delve into the detailed advice of Imam al-Juwayni on the etiquette of turn-taking (al-munāwaba) in debate. He emphasises patience even when the opponent's speech seems misguided, and provides a step-by-step protocol for handling disruptive interruptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Juwayni's basic rule
Read al-Juwayni's statement: both debaters have equal right to speak in turn; patience is required even if the speech seems like delusion.
The term 'waswās' suggests seemingly irrational talk.
2
Understand the rationale for patience
What reason does al-Juwayni give for listening to seemingly delusional speech? He says it may actually be the source of confusion, so patience is needed to grasp it fully.
3
Outline the response to interruption
If the opponent interrupts, what should the debater do? First tolerate and admonish; if the opponent persists, cut off the conversation.
This is a graduated response.
4
Justify the harsh measure
Al-Juwayni says cutting off the conversation is justified because the goal is truth and enjoining good, which cannot be achieved with interruption. Even if onlookers think it is a loss.

Defining al-Mujmal in Usul al-Fiqh
Definition of mujmal in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mujmal according to the usuliyyun and identify its core characteristic.

Core Idea
A mujmal word has two possible meanings with no sign of preference.

In this passage, the author begins by defining a key technical term: al-mujmal. Understanding this definition is crucial because it determines when a text requires external explanation (bayan) from the Prophet. Without this definition, you cannot evaluate whether a legal argument based on an ambiguous text is sound.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence where the author defines mujmal. It is: 'اللفظ المجمل بأنه : ما لَهُ دلالة على أحد أمرين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر بالنسبة إليه'
The definition is explicit.
2
Break down the definition
Identify the three components: the word has an indication (dalaala), the indication points to one of two matters, and there is no advantage (maziyya) for one over the other relative to the word itself.
The key is the lack of preference.
3
Distinguish from general ('amm)
Note that the objector later claims the word is general, not ambiguous. A general word ('amm) covers all instances of a category, while a mujmal word is ambiguous between two distinct possibilities.
This distinction is central to the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالمجمل
يعرف الأصوليون اللفظ المجمل بأنه : ما لَهُ دلالة على أحد أمرين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر بالنسبة إليه»
فالمستدل قد يستدل بالآية ويدعي فيها الإجمال لحاجته إلى ذلك، وهو أن يرى المستدل أن أفعال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لا تدل بنفسها على الوجوب، لكن إذا أخرجها النبي الله مخرج البيان كانت دالة على الوجوب . مثال ذلك : أن يستدل من يقول بهذه القاعدة من علماء المالكية على أن الإحرام بقوله الله الأكبر، لا يجوز ولا يجزئ من ذلك إلا الله أكبر»، فيستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ ) ، ويدعي في ذلك الإجمال وأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فسر هذا المجمل بفعله والذي كان يفتتح به الله أكبر، وبيان المجمل الواجب هو على الوجوب، فوجب أن يكون هذا اللفظ هو الواجب فلا يجزي غيره.
والاعتراض عليه إنما يكون من وجهين : الأول : أن يمنع المعترض كون الآية مجملة، فيقول : إنها ليست بمجملة، بل عامة، لأن الصلاة في كلام العرب هي الدعاء وإنما أضيف إليه شروط، وذلك لا يخرجه عن موضوعه كما لا يخرج اشتراط الطهارة لمس المصحف عن موضوعه، فوجب أن يحمل ذلك على كل دعاء إلا ما خصه الدليل . والطريق في الجواب على هذا الوجه من الاعتراض ؛ أن يقرر المستدل ما ادعاه من الإجمال وينصره، بأن يقول : إن الصلاة في كلام العرب هي الدعاء، والمراد به في الشرع غير ما وضع له من أفعال وركوع وسجود، فلا يعلم المراد بها من اللفظ، وافتقر في بيانه إلى غيره وهذا بمنزلة قوله تعالى : ﴿وَءَاتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حصَادِهِه . . . (1) ، لما لم يفهم المراد من لفظه وافتقر في بيانه إلى غيره، اتفق على إجماله، فكذلك في مسألتنا مثله . والوجه الثاني من الاعتراض: أن يمنع كون الخبر بيانا للآية، بأن يقول : لا أسلم أن ما ورد عن النبي بيان للآية، بل يجوز أن يكون بيانا، ويجوز أن يكون فعلا مبتدأ على وجه الاستحباب، وإذا احتمل الأمرين بطلت دعوى البيان، ولو كان بيانا للأمر لوجب جميع أفعاله لأنه خرج على مخرج واحد، ولما لم يجب الجميع دل أنه ليس على وجه البيان.
والمسلك بالنسبة للمستدل في الجواب عن هذا الوجه من الاعتراض أن يقول : إذا ثبت بما ذكرنا أن الآية مجملة وأنها مفتقرة إلى البيان وفعل ما فعله باسم أن يكون بيانا للصلاة المجملة المأمور بها، ولا يلزم أن يكون الصلاة، وجب جميع ما فعله واجبًا لأننا لو تركنا ذلك الظاهر لحملنا الكل على الوجوب، ولكنه دل الدليل على العدول عن الظاهر في بعض الأفعال، وبقي الباقي يحمل على ظاهره
Fourth Topic: Objection to reasoning with the ambiguous (mujmal). The usuliyyun define the ambiguous word as: that which has an indication to one of two matters with no advantage of one over the other relative to it. The proponent may argue with a verse and claim ambiguity in it due to his need for that. That is, the proponent sees that the actions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, do not by themselves indicate obligation, but if the Prophet presents them as an explanation (bayan), they indicate obligation. An example of that: one who holds this principle among the Malikis argues that entering ihram with saying 'Allahu Akbar' is not permissible and nothing suffices except 'Allahu Akbar'. He argues with the Almighty's saying: 'Establish prayer', and claims ambiguity in that, and that the Prophet explained this ambiguous (mujmal) by his action of opening with 'Allahu Akbar', and the explanation of the ambiguous is obligatory, so this word must be obligatory and nothing else suffices. The objection to it comes from two aspects: First: the objector denies that the verse is ambiguous, saying it is not ambiguous but general, because 'prayer' in the Arabs' speech is supplication, and conditions have been added to it, and that does not remove it from its subject matter just as requiring purity for touching the Mushaf does not remove it from its subject matter. So it must be interpreted as every supplication except what the evidence specifies. The method of answering this aspect of objection is that the proponent affirms his claimed ambiguity and supports it by saying: 'Prayer' in the Arabs' speech is supplication, and what is intended by it in the law is other than its literal meaning of actions, bowing, and prostration, so what is intended by it is not known from the word, and it needs an explanation from something else. This is like the Almighty's saying: 'And give its due on the day of its harvest', since the intended meaning was not understood from its wording and it needed an explanation from something else, they agreed on its ambiguity. Similarly in our issue. The second aspect of objection: denying that the hadith is an explanation (bayan) of the verse, by saying: I do not accept that what came from the Prophet is an explanation of the verse; rather it could be an explanation, and it could be an independent action on the basis of recommendation. If it is possible for both, the claim of explanation is invalidated. If it were an explanation of the command, all his actions would have been obligatory because they came on a single basis, and since all are not obligatory, it indicates it is not as an explanation. The course for the proponent in answering this aspect of objection is to say: If it is established by what we mentioned that the verse is ambiguous and that it requires explanation, and the action he did is as an explanation of the ambiguous commanded prayer, then it is necessary that all his actions be obligatory, because if we abandon that apparent meaning we would interpret everything as obligatory, but evidence indicates deviation from the apparent in some actions, and the rest remains interpreted according to its apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the method of objecting to arguments that rely on ambiguous (mujmal) texts. The author begins by defining mujmal according to the usuliyyun: it is a word that indicates one of two possibilities with no preference for either. Then he provides an example from Maliki jurisprudence: a proponent argues that the phrase 'Allahu Akbar' is obligatory for entering ihram because the Quranic command 'Establish prayer' is mujmal, and the Prophet explained it by starting with 'Allahu Akbar'. The objector then has two possible lines of attack. The first objection is to deny the ambiguity of the verse, claiming that 'prayer' in Arabic means supplication, and the added conditions do not change its literal sense, so it remains general. The proponent's response is to affirm the ambiguity by showing that the legal meaning of prayer (specific actions) is not understood from the word alone, similar to the verse 'Give its due on the day of harvest' which is ambiguous and required explanation. The second objection is to deny that the prophetic action is an explanation of the verse; it could be an independent recommended act. The proponent's response is that if the verse is ambiguous and requires explanation, the prophetic action must be explanatory, and the fact that not all prophetic actions are obligatory is due to other evidence specifying certain exceptions, while the rest remain obligatory as per the apparent meaning. The commentary follows the author's order: definition, example, two objections, and their respective responses.

Key Terms
المجمل
البيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining feature of a mujmal word?
A) It is a word that is difficult to understand.
B) It has indication to one of two matters with no advantage of one over the other relative to it.
C) It is a word that requires external interpretation because it is too long.
D) It is a word that has multiple meanings all equally valid.

Key Takeaway
A mujmal word is one that points to two possible meanings equally, requiring external explanation to resolve the ambiguity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following text is mujmal according to the definition: 'Give its due on the day of harvest.' Explain why or why not based on the definition.

The First Objection: Denying Ambiguity
First objection: denying the verse is mujmal, and the response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first objection to a mujmal argument and explain the proponent's response.

Core Idea
The objector may deny that the text is mujmal, claiming it is general ('amm) instead.

After defining mujmal and giving an example, the author presents two ways an opponent can object to an argument based on a mujmal text. The first is to deny the ambiguity altogether, asserting that the text is actually general. Understanding this objection and its response is essential for anyone engaging in usul al-fiqh debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the sentence beginning with 'الوجه الأول': the objector says the verse is not mujmal but 'amma (general).
The objector uses the fact that 'prayer' in Arabic means supplication and conditions don't change its essence.
2
Examine the objector's reasoning
The objector argues that the word 'prayer' in Arabic is supplication, and the added conditions (like bowing) do not remove it from its subject matter. Therefore it is general covering all supplications unless specified otherwise.
This is a linguistic argument.
3
Analyze the proponent's response
The proponent (mustadill) responds by affirming the ambiguity: 'prayer' in law refers to specific acts different from its linguistic meaning, so the intended meaning is unknown from the word alone. He compares it to the verse 'Give its due on the day of harvest' which is agreed to be mujmal.
The response uses analogy to establish the same type of ambiguity.
4
Compare to the definition
Check whether the proponent's response aligns with the definition of mujmal: the word points to two things (linguistic vs legal meaning) with no preference.
This shows the definition in action.

المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالمجمل
يعرف الأصوليون اللفظ المجمل بأنه : ما لَهُ دلالة على أحد أمرين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر بالنسبة إليه»
فالمستدل قد يستدل بالآية ويدعي فيها الإجمال لحاجته إلى ذلك، وهو أن يرى المستدل أن أفعال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لا تدل بنفسها على الوجوب، لكن إذا أخرجها النبي الله مخرج البيان كانت دالة على الوجوب . مثال ذلك : أن يستدل من يقول بهذه القاعدة من علماء المالكية على أن الإحرام بقوله الله الأكبر، لا يجوز ولا يجزئ من ذلك إلا الله أكبر»، فيستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ ) ، ويدعي في ذلك الإجمال وأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فسر هذا المجمل بفعله والذي كان يفتتح به الله أكبر، وبيان المجمل الواجب هو على الوجوب، فوجب أن يكون هذا اللفظ هو الواجب فلا يجزي غيره.

(1) ، لما لم يفهم المراد من لفظه وافتقر في بيانه إلى غيره، اتفق على إجماله، فكذلك في مسألتنا مثله . والوجه الثاني من الاعتراض: أن يمنع كون الخبر بيانا للآية، بأن يقول : لا أسلم أن ما ورد عن النبي بيان للآية، بل يجوز أن يكون بيانا، ويجوز أن يكون فعلا مبتدأ على وجه الاستحباب، وإذا احتمل الأمرين بطلت دعوى البيان، ولو كان بيانا للأمر لوجب جميع أفعاله لأنه خرج على مخرج واحد، ولما لم يجب الجميع دل أنه ليس على وجه البيان.

Fourth Topic: Objection to reasoning with the ambiguous (mujmal). The usuliyyun define the ambiguous word as: that which has an indication to one of two matters with no advantage of one over the other relative to it. The proponent may argue with a verse and claim ambiguity in it due to his need for that. That is, the proponent sees that the actions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, do not by themselves indicate obligation, but if the Prophet presents them as an explanation (bayan), they indicate obligation. So it must be interpreted as every supplication except what the evidence specifies. The method of answering this aspect of objection is that the proponent affirms his claimed ambiguity and supports it by saying: 'Prayer' in the Arabs' speech is supplication, and what is intended by it in the law is other than its literal meaning of actions, bowing, and prostration, so what is intended by it is not known from the word, and it needs an explanation from something else. The second aspect of objection: denying that the hadith is an explanation (bayan) of the verse, by saying: I do not accept that what came from the Prophet is an explanation of the verse; rather it could be an explanation, and it could be an independent action on the basis of recommendation. If it is possible for both, the claim of explanation is invalidated. If it were an explanation of the command, all his actions would have been obligatory because they came on a single basis, and since all are not obligatory, it indicates it is not as an explanation. Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the method of objecting to arguments that rely on ambiguous (mujmal) texts. The author begins by defining mujmal according to the usuliyyun: it is a word that indicates one of two possibilities with no preference for either. Then he provides an example from Maliki jurisprudence: a proponent argues that the phrase 'Allahu Akbar' is obligatory for entering ihram because the Quranic command 'Establish prayer' is mujmal, and the Prophet explained it by starting with 'Allahu Akbar'. The second objection is to deny that the prophetic action is an explanation of the verse; it could be an independent recommended act. Key Takeaway
