The Second Objection: Denying the Explanation
Second objection: denying the prophetic action is a bayan, and the response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the second objection to a mujmal argument and explain the proponent's response.

Core Idea
The objector may accept the ambiguity but deny that the prophetic action is an explanation (bayan).

The second objection targets the link between the claimed mujmal text and the prophetic action. Even if the verse is ambiguous, the objector argues that the Prophet's action might not be an explanation but an independent recommended act. Understanding how to counter this objection is vital for establishing that the action carries the ruling of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second objection
Find the phrase 'الوجه الثاني من الاعتراض' and read the objector's argument: he does not accept that the prophetic action is a bayan of the verse; it could be an independent mustahabb act.
The objector allows that the prophetic action could be either explanatory or independent.
2
Examine the objector's reasoning
The objector argues that if the action were an explanation, then all prophetic actions would be equally obligatory because they would all be explanations of the same command. Since not all his actions are obligatory, it is not necessarily an explanation.
This uses the principle of uniform treatment.
3
Analyze the proponent's response
The proponent responds that once the ambiguity is established and the Prophet's action is seen as explanatory, then the default is that his action indicates obligation. However, evidence (dalil) may indicate a deviation from the apparent in some cases, but the rest remain obligatory according to the apparent meaning.
The response uses the concept of 'apparent meaning' being obligation unless there is a specific counter-indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالمجمل
يعرف الأصوليون اللفظ المجمل بأنه : ما لَهُ دلالة على أحد أمرين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر بالنسبة إليه»
فالمستدل قد يستدل بالآية ويدعي فيها الإجمال لحاجته إلى ذلك، وهو أن يرى المستدل أن أفعال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لا تدل بنفسها على الوجوب، لكن إذا أخرجها النبي الله مخرج البيان كانت دالة على الوجوب . مثال ذلك : أن يستدل من يقول بهذه القاعدة من علماء المالكية على أن الإحرام بقوله الله الأكبر، لا يجوز ولا يجزئ من ذلك إلا الله أكبر»، فيستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ ) ، ويدعي في ذلك الإجمال وأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فسر هذا المجمل بفعله والذي كان يفتتح به الله أكبر، وبيان المجمل الواجب هو على الوجوب، فوجب أن يكون هذا اللفظ هو الواجب فلا يجزي غيره.

Fourth Topic: Objection to reasoning with the ambiguous (mujmal). The usuliyyun define the ambiguous word as: that which has an indication to one of two matters with no advantage of one over the other relative to it. The proponent may argue with a verse and claim ambiguity in it due to his need for that. That is, the proponent sees that the actions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, do not by themselves indicate obligation, but if the Prophet presents them as an explanation (bayan), they indicate obligation. An example of that: one who holds this principle among the Malikis argues that entering ihram with saying 'Allahu Akbar' is not permissible and nothing suffices except 'Allahu Akbar'. The objection to it comes from two aspects: First: the objector denies that the verse is ambiguous, saying it is not ambiguous but general, because 'prayer' in the Arabs' speech is supplication, and conditions have been added to it, and that does not remove it from its subject matter just as requiring purity for touching the Mushaf does not remove it from its subject matter. So it must be interpreted as every supplication except what the evidence specifies. The method of answering this aspect of objection is that the proponent affirms his claimed ambiguity and supports it by saying: 'Prayer' in the Arabs' speech is supplication, and what is intended by it in the law is other than its literal meaning of actions, bowing, and prostration, so what is intended by it is not known from the word, and it needs an explanation from something else. Similarly in our issue. The second aspect of objection: denying that the hadith is an explanation (bayan) of the verse, by saying: I do not accept that what came from the Prophet is an explanation of the verse; rather it could be an explanation, and it could be an independent action on the basis of recommendation. If it is possible for both, the claim of explanation is invalidated. If it were an explanation of the command, all his actions would have been obligatory because they came on a single basis, and since all are not obligatory, it indicates it is not as an explanation. The course for the proponent in answering this aspect of objection is to say: If it is established by what we mentioned that the verse is ambiguous and that it requires explanation, and the action he did is as an explanation of the ambiguous commanded prayer, then it is necessary that all his actions be obligatory, because if we abandon that apparent meaning we would interpret everything as obligatory, but evidence indicates deviation from the apparent in some actions, and the rest remains interpreted according to its apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the method of objecting to arguments that rely on ambiguous (mujmal) texts. The author begins by defining mujmal according to the usuliyyun: it is a word that indicates one of two possibilities with no preference for either. Then he provides an example from Maliki jurisprudence: a proponent argues that the phrase 'Allahu Akbar' is obligatory for entering ihram because the Quranic command 'Establish prayer' is mujmal, and the Prophet explained it by starting with 'Allahu Akbar'. The objector then has two possible lines of attack. The first objection is to deny the ambiguity of the verse, claiming that 'prayer' in Arabic means supplication, and the added conditions do not change its literal sense, so it remains general. The proponent's response is to affirm the ambiguity by showing that the legal meaning of prayer (specific actions) is not understood from the word alone, similar to the verse 'Give its due on the day of harvest' which is ambiguous and required explanation. The second objection is to deny that the prophetic action is an explanation of the verse; it could be an independent recommended act. The proponent's response is that if the verse is ambiguous and requires explanation, the prophetic action must be explanatory, and the fact that not all prophetic actions are obligatory is due to other evidence specifying certain exceptions, while the rest remain obligatory as per the apparent meaning. The commentary follows the author's order: definition, example, two objections, and their respective responses.

Key Terms
البيان
الاستحباب
الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second objection, what is the objector's main reason for denying that the Prophet's action is a bayan?
A) The verse is not mujmal.
B) The Prophet's action could be an independent recommended act, and if it were a bayan, all his actions would be equally obligatory, which they are not.
C) The Prophet's action is not authentic.
D) The Maliki school does not consider it a bayan.

Key Takeaway
The second objection is that the prophetic action might not be an explanation but an independent act; the response is that the default is obligation unless specific evidence overrides it.

Construct a similar objection and response for another hypothetical: The verse 'And perform the pilgrimage' is claimed to be mujmal, and the Prophet performed Hajj in a certain way. Formulate the second objection (denying that his actions are an explanation) and the proponent's response.

Third Objection: Action Implies Obligation (Maliki vs Hanafi)
Author describes the third type of objection: opponent claims action implies obligation, with Maliki-Hanafi debate on wiping head.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical structure of the Maliki-Hanafi debate on whether prophetic actions indicate obligation or recommendation, and identify the key premises and responses in the exchange.

Core Idea
Prophetic actions: obligation or recommendation? The debate hinges on default implication of the Prophet's commands and actions.

In this lesson, we examine the third type of objection in jadal: when an opponent argues that a prophetic action is accompanied by an explicit command, thus implying obligation. The author illustrates this with a famous Maliki-Hanafi dispute over wiping the entire head in wudu. Understanding this debate is crucial for distinguishing how different schools derive rulings from prophetic practice and how they respond to objections.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim and the objection
Identify what each party asserts: the Maliki asserts wujub (obligation) based on the Prophet's action; the Hanafi objects that prophetic actions indicate istihbab (recommendation). Mark where the objection begins and ends in the passage.
The objection is signaled by 'فيعترضه الحنفي بأن يقول'
2
Pinpoint the key terms
Understand wujub vs istihbab, and mujmal (ambiguous text). Note how these technical terms from usul al-fiqh drive the argument. The Maliki claims the Prophet's action explains an obligatory mujmal.
The term 'المجمل' appears in the Maliki response.
3
Trace the Maliki's supporting arguments
The Maliki offers two proofs: (a) the general Quranic command to follow the Prophet (وَاتَّبِعُوهُ), implying his actions are binding; (b) the action clarifies the mujmal command in the hadith about purification ('No prayer is accepted without purification'). He also rebuts the Hanafi's exception by noting that some actions are recommended but the default is obligation.
Look for the phrase 'وهذا بيان للمجمل' and the analogy to commands.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
Evaluate whether the Maliki's response successfully counters the Hanafi objection. The Maliki establishes that even if some actions are proven recommended, the default remains obligation unless evidence indicates otherwise. The Hanafi objection does not overturn the default.
The author implies the Maliki position is defensible by providing a structured rebuttal.

Source Text

والثالث : أن يقول : قد اقترن به أمر وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام (صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي)، والأمر يقتضي الوجوب، ومثاله كذلك : استدلال المالكي في وجوب استيعاب الرأس في الوضوء بما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه توضأ فمسح رأسه بيديه فأقبل بهما وأدبر ، بدأ بمقدم رأسه حتى ذهب بهما إلى قفاه ثم ردهما إلى المكان الذي بدأ منه ، وأفعاله تقتضي الوجوب فيعترضه الحنفي بأن يقول : هذا لا يصح الاحتجاج به، لأن أفعال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نسلم أنها تقتضي الوجوب، وإنما تقتضي الاستحباب ومسح كل الرأس عندنا مستحب وليس بواجب. والجواب أن يقول المالكي : إن أفعال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عندنا على الوجوب، بدليل : قوله تعالى : ﴿ ... وَاتَّبِعُوهُ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَهْتَدُونَ ... ﴾، ولا خلاف أن أفعاله إذا كانت بيانا للمجمل الواجب إنما تكون على الوجوب وهذا بيان للمجمل من قوله : (لا تقبل صلاة بغير طهور)، وفي رواية: (لا يقبل الله صلاة بغير طهور). وكذلك فإنه إذا دل الدليل على أن بعض أفعاله في الوضوء على الاستحباب، لا ذلك يمنع من حمل سائرها عند الإطلاق على الوجوب، فإن بعض أوامره تحمل - بدليل - على الندب، وإن كان سائرها يحمل الإطلاق على الوجوب، وكذلك فإن بعض أفعاله في الصلاة يحمل على الندب بدليل، وإن كان سائرها يحمل على الوجوب عند عدم دليل الندب بما روي عنه أنه قال : (صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي).
And the third [type of objection]: that he says: 'An command is associated with it, which is his statement, peace be upon him: 'Pray as you have seen me praying.' And the command necessitates obligation. And an example of that as well: the Maliki's inference for the obligation of encompassing the head in ablution based on what is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he performed ablution and wiped his head with his hands, bringing them forward and backward, starting from the front of his head until he reached the back of his neck, then returned them to the place from which he started. And his actions necessitate obligation. So the Hanafi objects by saying: 'This cannot be used as evidence, because the actions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, we do not concede that they necessitate obligation; rather they necessitate recommendation, and wiping the entire head in our view is recommended and not obligatory.' The Maliki's response is to say: 'The actions of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in our view indicate obligation, based on the evidence of His statement: '...and follow him that you may be guided.' There is no disagreement that his actions, when they are an explanation of an obligatory ambiguous [text], are indeed indicative of obligation. And this is an explanation of the ambiguous text from his statement: 'No prayer is accepted without purification,' and in another narration: 'Allah does not accept prayer without purification.' Similarly, if evidence indicates that some of his actions in ablution are recommended, that does not prevent carrying the rest of them, when unrestricted, to obligation. For some of his commands are carried, by evidence, to recommendation, while the rest of them are carried, when unrestricted, to obligation. Similarly, some of his actions in prayer are carried to recommendation by evidence, while the rest are carried to obligation when there is no evidence of recommendation, based on what is narrated from him: 'Pray as you have seen me praying.'
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange presenting the third type of objection in jadal: when an opponent argues that the Prophet's action is accompanied by an explicit command that implies obligation. The author provides an example from the Maliki school: Malikis argue that wiping the entire head in wudu is obligatory because the Prophet's action of wiping his head demonstrates a command. The Hanafi objects that prophetic actions only indicate recommendation (istihbab), not obligation. The Maliki responds with two arguments: first, the Quranic command to follow the Prophet implies that his actions are binding; second, if some actions are known to be recommended, that does not negate that other actions retain obligation. The Maliki further analogizes to commands: some commands may be interpreted as recommended, but the default is obligation. This passage illustrates how school disputes rely on differing principles of interpretation regarding prophetic actions and commands.

الوجوب
المجمل

Key Takeaway
The default implication of prophetic actions is a key point of ijtihadi dispute; the Maliki relies on the principle that actions explaining an obligatory ambiguous text are themselves obligatory.

Refuting the Cause of Riba by Counterexample
Author argues that weight is not the cause of riba by counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a categorical syllogism from a usul al-fiqh argument and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
A valid counterexample can refute a proposed effective cause in Islamic law.

In this passage, the author dismantles a proposed 'illa (cause) for riba by showing it leads to a contradiction with an established ruling. The reasoning is a crisp categorical syllogism. By learning to identify its structure, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion of the argument
Read the passage and locate the statement that the author is trying to prove. In this case, it is that weight is not the cause of riba in gold and silver. This is the conclusion (natija).
The conclusion is often signalled by words like 'فثبت' (so it is established).
2
Find the major premise
Look for a general rule that connects the cause to a prohibition. Here: 'كل شيئين جمعتهما علة واحدة في الربا لم يجز إسلام أحدهما في الآخر'. This is the major premise (al-muqaddima al-kubra).
The major premise is a universal conditional: if two items share a cause, they cannot be exchanged.
3
Find the minor premise
The minor premise states a specific case that falls under the major premise. Here: 'فلما اجتمعنا على جواز إسلام الذهب والفضة في الموزونات' — we have agreed that gold and silver can be exchanged for weighed items. Note that this is the opposite of what the rule would predict.
The minor premise is a fact about the permissibility of a specific exchange.
4
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism
Write the argument in standard form: Major: All pairs sharing a single riba cause cannot be exchanged. Minor: Gold and silver can be exchanged with weighed items. Conclusion: Therefore, gold and silver do not share a single riba cause with weighed items. Note this is a second figure syllogism (Camestres).
The middle term is 'sharing a single riba cause'; the conclusion negates that.
5
Verify validity and application
Check that the syllogism is valid: If the major and minor are true, the conclusion follows necessarily. Since the minor is an established ruling, the only way to avoid contradiction is to reject the proposed cause (weight). Thus the argument refutes the claim.
The argument is formally valid and sound given the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموزونات يجري للوزن لما جاز إسلام الدراهم والدنانير فيها، لأن كل شيئين جمعتهما علة واحدة في الربا لم يجز إسلام أحدهما في الآخر، كالذهب والفضة، فلما اجتمعنا على جواز إسلام الذهب والفضة في الموزونات، دل على أن الوزن ليس بعلة للربا فيهما، فثبت أن علة الربا ما ذكرنا
Weighed items: reasoning from weight because it is permitted to sell dirhams and dinars in exchange for them, because every two things that are united by a single cause in riba, it is not permitted to exchange one for the other, like gold and silver. When we agreed upon the permissibility of exchanging gold and silver for weighed items, this indicates that weight is not the cause of riba in them. So it is established that the cause of riba is what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument to disprove a proposed cause ('illa) for riba. The argument uses a categorical syllogism in the second figure. The opponent claims that weight (al-wazn) is the cause of riba in gold and silver. The author refutes this by noting that it is permissible to exchange gold and silver for weighed items (al-mawzunat) in a salam contract. If weight were the sole cause, then gold and silver, sharing that cause, could not be exchanged for other weighed items. Since exchange is permitted, weight cannot be the cause. The argument assumes the principle: any two items sharing a single riba cause cannot be exchanged. Gold and silver are exchangeable with weighed items, so they do not share that cause. The reasoning is sound and follows the pattern of a categorical syllogism: major premise (all pairs with a single cause cannot be exchanged), minor premise (gold and silver are exchangeable), conclusion (gold and silver do not have that single cause). This is a clear example of demonstrating the invalidity of an 'illa by showing it leads to a contradiction with established rulings.

الموزونات
إسلام الدراهم والدنانير

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism can refute a proposed 'illa in Islamic law by showing it leads to a contradiction with an established ruling.

But it is permitted to exchange gold for silver with delay. Therefore, interest is not the cause.' Reconstruct this as a valid syllogism.

Reasoning by Testimony of Principles (Shahadat al-Usul)
Author describes and illustrates shahadat al-usul with three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a shahadat al-usul argument and reconstruct the dialectical exchange using a fiqh example.

Core Idea
Shahadat al-usul proves an 'illa by showing it aligns with the consistent pattern of Sharia principles.

In this passage, the author explains the dialectical method of 'shahadat al-usul' (testimony of principles). This method is used in usul al-fiqh debates to defend a proposed 'illa (cause) by pointing to a consistent pattern across established rulings. Three examples from different madhhabs illustrate how the method works. You will learn to recognize the structure of such arguments and apply them in dialectical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed 'illa
Find the 'illa (cause) that the claimant puts forward for the ruling. In each example, the claimant states a general rule that connects the specific case to a principle.
2
Locate the objection
The objector asks for the proof (dalil) of the 'illa. Identify the exact question.
3
Examine the response using shahadat al-usul
The respondent answers by appealing to a consistent pattern observed in established usul (principles). Note how the response cites multiple cases that follow the same rule.
4
Assess the strength of the argument
The author notes that this method yields preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-zann), not certainty. Evaluate why it is considered a valid dialectical move.
The argument is not a categorical proof but an inductive inference from multiple instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - 2 - الدلالة بشهادة الأصول : وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في القيء : إنه لا يبطل الوضوء، لأن ما لا ينقض قليله الوضوء لم ينقض كثيره الوضوء، كالدمع والعرق . فيقال له : ما الدليل على صحة هذه العلة؟ فيقول مجيبا : الدليل هو شهادة الأصول، وذلك أن الأصول متفقة على التسوية بين القليل والكثير في ما ينقض وفي ما لا ينقض الوضوء، ألا ترى أن البول والغائط والنوم والمباشرة، لما نقض استوى قليله وكثيره؟ وما لا ينقض كالدمع والعرق والكلام يستوي فيه الكثير والكثير، فدل على ما قلناه ومثاله أيضًا : أن يقول المالكي في القهقهة : إنها لا تنقض الوضوء في الصلاة، لأن ما لا ينقض الوضوء خارج الصلاة لا ينقضه داخل الصلاة. فيقول الحنفي : ما الدليل على صحة هذه العلة؟ فيقول مجيبا : الدليل عليه أن أصول الشرع مبنية على أن ما لا ينقض الوضوء خارج الصلاة فإنه لا ينقضه داخل الصلاة، كالكلام والضحك واللعب والأكل وغير ذلك، وما ينقض الوضوء داخل الصلاة فإنه ينقضه خارج الصلاة كاللمس ومس الذكر والبول والغائط وسائر أنواع الأحداث، ولما رأينا القهقهة لا تنقض الوضوء خارج الصلاة، دل ذلك على أنها لا تنقضه في الصلاة ومثاله أيضًا أن يقول الحنبلي : لا تجب الزكاة في إناث الخيل، لأنها لا تجب في ذكورها كالبغال والحمير والفيلة وغير ذلك من الحيوانات وعكسه الإبل والبقر والغنم . فإن قيل له : ما الدليل على صحة هذه العلة؟ أجاب قائلا : إذا كانت الأصول مرتبة على التسوية بين الذكور والإناث في وجوب الزكاة وسقوطها ووجدنا الخيل لا زكاة في ذكورها إذا انفردت، بالإجماع، لم تجب في إناثها ، وكان ذلك طريقا يقتضي غلبة الظن، لأن الظن يمنع وجود الحكم في الغالب
B-2- Indication by testimony of principles: For example, al-Shafi'i says regarding vomiting: It does not invalidate wudu, because what does not invalidate wudu in small amount does not invalidate wudu in large amount, like tears and sweat. It is said to him: What is the proof of the soundness of this cause? He answers: The proof is the testimony of principles, and that is that the principles agree on equality between the small and the large in what invalidates and what does not invalidate wudu. Do you not see that urine, feces, sleep, and sexual touching, when they invalidate, their small and large are equal? And what does not invalidate, like tears, sweat, and speech, its large and small are equal. So it indicates what we said. Another example: al-Maliki says regarding loud laughter: It does not invalidate wudu in prayer, because what does not invalidate wudu outside prayer does not invalidate it inside prayer. The Hanafi says: What is the proof of the soundness of this cause? He answers: The proof is that the principles of the Sharia are based on the fact that what does not invalidate wudu outside prayer does not invalidate it inside prayer, like speech, smiling, playing, eating, etc., and what invalidates wudu inside prayer invalidates it outside prayer, like touching, touching the penis, urine, feces, and all kinds of impurities. Since we see that loud laughter does not invalidate wudu outside prayer, this indicates that it does not invalidate it in prayer. Another example: al-Hanbali says: Zakat is not obligatory on female horses, because it is not obligatory on their males, like mules, donkeys, elephants, and other animals; its opposite is camels, cows, and sheep. So if it is said to him: What is the proof of the soundness of this cause? He answers: When the principles are arranged on equality between males and females in the obligation and non-obligation of zakat, and we find that horses have no zakat on their males when alone, by consensus, then it is not obligatory on their females, and that is a way that necessitates preponderance of opinion, because opinion prevents the existence of the ruling in most cases.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of the method of 'shahadat al-usul' (testimony of principles). It is a dialectical technique used to justify a proposed 'illa (cause) by pointing to a consistent pattern in the established principles (usul) of the Sharia. The method is demonstrated through three examples from different madhhabs. First, al-Shafi'i argues that vomiting does not invalidate wudu regardless of quantity, basing this on the principle that in all established cases, little and much are treated equally: things that invalidate (like urine) do so in any amount; things that do not (like tears) are harmless in any amount. The objection asks for proof of this 'illa, and the response is that the usul agree on this equality. Second, al-Maliki argues that loud laughter in prayer does not invalidate wudu because it does not invalidate outside prayer; the Hanafi objects, and the answer is that the Sharia's principles (usul al-shar') consistently hold that whatever does not break wudu outside also does not break it inside, as seen with speech, smiling, etc. Third, al-Hanbali argues that female horses are not subject to zakat because males are not, and the objection is answered by referring to the principle that males and females are treated equally in zakat rulings across all animals. The text concludes that this reasoning yields preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-zann). Note that this method is not a formal syllogism but an analogical inference from a pattern in established rulings. It is considered a valid form of reasoning in jadal (dialectical argumentation).

Key Terms
شهادة الأصول
علة
التسوية بين القليل والكثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Shafi'i example regarding vomiting, what principle ('asl) is used to justify the 'illa?
A) The small and large of tears are equal.
B) The small and large of invalidating things are equal.
C) Vomiting does not invalidate wudu.
D) Sweat and tears are analogous.

Key Takeaway
Shahadat al-usul is a dialectical method that defends an 'illa by demonstrating its consistency with the established principles of Sharia, yielding preponderant opinion.

Understanding Lack of Effect (Adam al-Ta'thir) in Jadal
Defines and illustrates 'adam al-ta'thir with classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection of 'adam al-ta'thir and determine whether a description (wasf) is essential by checking if the ruling depends on it.

Core Idea
Lack of effect means the ruling exists without the claimed cause — the objection is valid only if removing the cause changes the ruling.

In jurisprudential debates, one common way to refute an opponent's causal claim is to show that the alleged cause has no real effect on the ruling. This is called 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect). The passage defines this concept and illustrates it with a classic example from Shafi'i about the sale of an absent item. Understanding this tool sharpens your ability to evaluate analogical arguments in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the three formulations of the definition
The author gives three equivalent ways to define lack of effect: (1) the ruling exists without the cause, (2) the ruling does not disappear when the cause is absent in some instance, (3) the ruling exists without the description in the very case where causality was claimed. These all highlight that the claimed cause is not actually influencing the ruling.
These phrasings help you recognise lack of effect in different argumentative contexts.
2
Analyse the Shafi'i example
Al-Shafi'i argues that selling an absent item is invalid because it is not visible, analogizing to a bird in the air. The objector retorts that even if the bird were visible, the sale would still be invalid because of inability to deliver. So invisibility has no effect — the ruling stays the same without it.
This shows that the description 'not visible' is not the real cause; the real cause is lack of delivery capability.
3
Distinguish essential vs. incidental descriptions
The text distinguishes two types of descriptions: those whose removal invalidates the cause (essential) and those whose removal does not (incidental). Only objections that target essential descriptions constitute valid 'adam al-ta'thir.
If an opponent objects using an incidental description, the objection fails because the cause would still stand without it.
4
Apply the distinction to a new case
Given a claimed cause for a ruling, ask: 'If I remove this description, does the ruling change?' If yes, the description is essential and the cause is vulnerable. If no, the cause is unaffected.
This is the core test for lack of effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : الاعتراض على العلة بعدم التأثير عدم التأثير، معناه : وجود الحكم مع عدم وجود العلة، أو هو ألا يعدم الحكم لعدم العلة في موضع من المواضع، أو هو وجود الحكم بدون الوصف في المحل الذي أثبتت عليته فيه . ومثاله : أن يقول الشافعي مستدلا على عدم صحة بيع الغائب : الغائب مبيع فلا غير مرئي يصح بيعه كالطير في الهواء .. فقد جعل العلة في عدم صحة بيع الطير في الهواء كونه غير مرئي، ليلحق بيع الغائب به في هذه العلة . هذه العلة . . فيقول المعترض له هذه العلة لا تأثير لها في الأصل لأن عدم الصحة يوجد في الطير ولو كان مرئيا، فإن الطير في الهواء لا يصح بيعه مطلقا مرئيا أو غير مرئي لعدم القدرة على تسليمه فالوصف غير مؤثر في الحكم لوجود الحكم بدونه والأوصاف على ضربين وصف إذا أسقط انتقضت العلة، وهذا الذي يخل بالعلة بعدم التأثير فيه والثاني ألا تنتقض العلة بإسقاطه، فهذا لا يخل بالعلة عدم التأثير فيه . أ - الاعتراض بعدم التأثير في الوصف الذي تنتقض العلة بإسقاطه : وهو على وجهين : أ - -1- أن يكون الوصف له تأثير على مذهب المستدل، والحكم يتعلق به عنده، ولكن لا تأثير له في الأصول : وذلك مثل أن يقول المالكي : إن من جهر
The second demand: Objection to the cause by lack of effect. Lack of effect means: the existence of the ruling without the existence of the cause, or that the ruling is not absent due to the absence of the cause in some instance, or the existence of the ruling without the description in the place where its causality was established. Its example: al-Shafi'i, providing evidence for the invalidity of the sale of an absent item, says: The absent item is a commodity that is not visible, so its sale is invalid like a bird in the air. So he made the cause for the invalidity of the sale of a bird in the air its being not visible, in order to attach the sale of the absent item to it in this cause. The objector says to him: This cause has no effect in the original case, because invalidity exists in the bird even if it were visible. For the bird in the air is absolutely not sold, whether visible or not visible, due to the inability to deliver it. So the description is not effective in the ruling because the ruling exists without it. And descriptions are of two types: a description which, if removed, the cause is invalidated – this is what compromises the cause by lack of effect in it; and the second is that the cause is not invalidated by its removal – this does not compromise the cause by lack of effect. A - Objection by lack of effect in the description that compromises the cause by its removal: This has two aspects: A-1- That the description has an effect according to the proponent’s doctrine, and the ruling is attached to it according to him, but it has no effect in the principles: for example, al-Maliki says that he who makes it ...
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage defines the objection known as 'adam al-ta'thir' (lack of effect). It is a type of refutation in jadal where the opponent shows that the proposed 'illa (cause) has no real effect on the ruling. The definition is given in three equivalent phrasings: (1) the ruling exists without the cause, (2) the ruling does not disappear when the cause is absent in some instance, (3) the ruling exists without the description in the very case where the cause was claimed. The example: al-Shafi'i argues that the sale of an absent item (al-gha'ib) is invalid because it is not visible, analogizing to a bird in the air. The objector points out that the invalidity of selling a bird in the air is not due to invisibility; even if the bird were visible, the sale would still be invalid because the buyer cannot take delivery. Thus the description (invisibility) has no effect. The text then classifies descriptions into two types: those whose removal invalidates the cause (i.e., they are essential), and those whose removal does not affect the cause (i.e., they are incidental). Only the first type leads to a valid objection of lack of effect. Further, the first type is subdivided; here the author begins the first subdivision: where the description is effective in the proponent's own madhhab but has no effect in the usul (principles of Sharia). An example from al-Maliki is given but cut off. The classification is meticulous and helps students identify exactly when an objection is valid.

الوصف
انتقاض العلة

A) The item is absent.
B) The item is a bird in the air.
C) The item is not visible.
D) The item cannot be delivered.

Key Takeaway
Lack of effect is a powerful jadal tool: it exposes that the alleged cause does not actually influence the ruling, but only works if the description is essential to the ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following claim: 'Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant. Nabidh is intoxicating, so it is also forbidden.' Construct an 'adam al-ta'thir objection showing that intoxication is not the effect, because a non-intoxicating drink could still be forbidden (e.g., if it is impure).
?
Identify whether the description 'being a predator' is essential for the prohibition of eating a predatory animal. If the ruling remains when the description is removed (e.g., a non-predatory animal can also be forbidden), then the description is incidental and the objection fails.

The Correct Break (Kasr) in Descriptions and Substitutes
Author illustrates a correct kasr with an example from tayammum and intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a correct kasr in a jadal exchange by demonstrating that a substitute description has a legal effect the original lacks.

Core Idea
A correct kasr proves the substitute is not analogous because it has a different legal effect.

In jadal, the objector can break the opponent's analogy (kasr) by showing a relevant difference. This passage from Usul al-Fiqh demonstrates a correct kasr: the description of being a substitute (badal) is not effective in removing intention, because substitutes often carry additional legal requirements. The example of tayammum and wudu is used to argue that tayammum requires intention while wudu does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's analogy
Find the case the opponent is trying to analogize. Here, the opponent likely claims that wudu and tayammum are analogous regarding intention: both are forms of purification that do not require intention.
2
Find the objector's description (wasf)
The objector attacks by focusing on the description that makes the cases analogous. In the passage, the description is 'being a substitute for water' (tayammum is a substitute for wudu).
3
Test the effectiveness of the description
Ask: does this description actually necessitate the same ruling? The author argues it does not, because in the matter of intention, origins and substitutes can differ. He gives the example of expiations (kafarat) and counting (ʿadad) where they are equal, but then shows a counterexample where they differ.
4
Provide a counterexample
The author uses the fiqh example of staying overnight at Muzdalifah (origin, no intention) versus sending a sacrificial animal (substitute, requires intention). This shows that substitutes can have additional requirements.
5
Conclude the kasr is correct
Since the description 'being a substitute' does not remove intention in all cases, the analogy is broken. The objector has proven that the substitute has an effect that the origin lacks, making the kasr valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كسر صحيح، لأن الوصف الذي احترز به من التيمم غير مؤثر في إسقاط النية، ألا ترى أن الأصول والأبدال في باب النية في الشرع واحد، يدلك عليه أن الكفارات لما افتقرت إلى النية استوى أصلها والعدد لما لم تفتقر إلى النية، استوى أصلها وبدلها والطريق في الجواب أن يبين أن للبدل من التأثير في إيجاب النية ما ليس لأصل، ألا ترى أن المبيت بمزدلفة لما كان أصلا لم يفتقر إلى النية والهدي الذي يخرجه لما كان بدلا افتقر إلى النية، فجاز أن يكون الوضوء في مسألتنا أيضًا لما كان أصلا يراد للصلاة لم يفتقر إلى النية، والتيمم لما كان بدلا افتقر إلى النية.
A correct break (kasar), because the description that was guarded against in tayammum is not effective in removing the intention. Do you not see that origins and substitutes in the matter of intention in law are one? That is indicated by the fact that expiations, when they required intention, their origin and substitute were equal; and [acts like] counting, when they did not require intention, their origin and substitute were equal. The way in the answer is to show that the substitute has an effect in necessitating intention that the origin does not have. Do you not see that staying overnight at Muzdalifah, because it was an origin, did not require intention, while the sacrificial animal that [the pilgrim] sends out, because it was a substitute, required intention? So it is possible that ablution in our case also, because it is an origin intended for prayer, does not require intention, and tayammum, because it is a substitute, requires intention.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage defines a correct kasr (objection by breaking the analogy) through a specific fiqh example. The author begins by stating that the kasr is correct because the description (being a substitute for water) is not effective in removing the requirement of intention. He then explains the general principle: in the matter of intention, origins and substitutes are treated equally in law, as seen with expiations (kafarat) and counting (ʿadad). However, the correct answer for the objector is to demonstrate that the substitute may have an effect that the origin lacks. He gives the example of staying overnight at Muzdalifah (origin) not requiring intention, while the sacrificial animal sent out (substitute) does require intention. He applies this to the case at hand: wuduʾ (origin) does not require intention, but tayammum (substitute) does. The passage teaches that a correct kasr must show a difference in effectiveness between the original and the substitute description.

D) Because the objector failed to prove a difference.

Key Takeaway
A correct kasr in jadal requires proving that the description used in the analogy has no effective role in the ruling, often by showing that substitutes have different legal effects than their originals.

Understanding Isqat Wasf Mu'aththir in Jadal
Author explains the objection of dropping an effective description from the cause, with two fiqh examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an opponent drops an effective description in a jadal exchange and construct a valid response showing the description's differential impact.

Core Idea
Not every dropped description breaks an analogy; the key is whether the description is equally effective in both cases.

The objection of 'dropping an effective description' (Isqat Wasf Mu'aththir) occurs when an opponent claims that a description in the original case has been omitted from the new case, supposedly making the analogy invalid. However, the proponent can respond by showing that the description carries different weight in the two situations, thus preserving the analogy. The author provides two classic fiqh examples to illustrate this dynamic. Mastering this objection is crucial for engaging in disciplined juristic debate (munazara).

Guided Path
1
Grasp the concept of Isqat Wasf Mu'aththir
Understand that this objection targets an analogy by claiming that a key description (wasf) present in the original case (asl) has been dropped in the new case (far'), so the analogy fails. The response must demonstrate that the description is not equally effective in both contexts.
The opponent's goal is to show that the ruling (hukm) attached to the cause ('illah) does not transfer.
2
Analyze the first example: marriage vs. sale
The Shafi'i argues that selling an unseen item is invalid due to unknown description, analogous to selling an unspecified garment. The opponent counters with marriage, where unknown description does not invalidate. Identify the description dropped: 'ignorance of description'. Then see how the Shafi'i responds by differentiating the effect of ignorance in sale vs. marriage via the option of rescission (khiyar).
The proponent shows that the description's effect is contextual; it is not a general rule.
3
Analyze the second example: fruit before ripeness vs. dried fruit
The Maliki argues that selling fruit before ripening is invalid, analogized to selling with a condition to leave (tabqiya). The Hanafi objects by dropping the description of growth; he compares to dried fruit (where growth has ceased). Identify the dropped description: 'growth with risk of blight'. The Maliki responds by arguing that growth before ripening involves gharar (risk) and the prophetic prohibition, unlike dried fruit where risk is absent.
The response emphasizes that the dropped description is not equally effective because the risk is different.
4
Evaluate the logic of the response
In both examples, the proponent does not deny that the description is present in the original; they show that the description has a different impact in the counterexample. This is the correct way to refute a kasr of this type: demonstrate that the description's effect varies with context.
The strength of the refutation depends on clearly showing the disparity in effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - 2 - إسقاط وصف مؤثر في الحكم الذي علق على العلة : ومثاله أن يقول الشافعي في بيع ما لم يره المشتري : إنه مبيع مجهول الصفة عند العاقد حال العقد، فلم يصح بيعه كما لو قال : بعتك ثوبًا، فيقول المخالف : هذا ينكسر به إذا تروج امرأة لم يرها، فإنها مجهولة الصفة عند العاقد حال العقد، ثم يصح. فيقول المستدل مجيبا : ليس النكاح كالبيع في هذا الحكم لأن للجهالة من التأثير في باب البيع ما ليس لها في النكاح، ولهذا لو تزوج امرأة لم يرها ثم رآها لم يثبت له الخيار ولو اشترى سلعة لم يرا ثم رآها لثبت له الخيار، فجاز أن يكون عدم الرؤية في النكاح لا يمنع الصحة، وفي البيع يمنع الصحة. ومثاله كذلك : أن يستدل المالكي على أن بيع الثمرة قبل بدو الصلاح على الإطلاق لا يجوز، لان هذه ثمرة نامية أفردت بالبيع قبل بدو الصلاح من غير شرط القطع، فوجب ألا يصح، كما لو اشترط التبقية، فيقول الحنفي : لا أن تفرد بالبيع قبل بدو الصلاح من غير شرط القطع، ومع لأنه أسقط كما لو جفت الأصول، فهذا الكسر ليس بصحيح، من العلة وصفا مؤثرا للحكم الذي علق عليه. والطريق في الجواب بالنسبة للمستدل : أن يبين فساد هذا النوع من الكسر ، ثم النماء مع التبقية غرر وتعريض تأثير الوصف الذي أسقط، فيقول: إن وجود النماء مع التبقية غرر وتعريض للعاهة، ولذلك نهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن بيع الثمار حتى تزهي أي تحمر وحتى تنجو العاهة، فإذا أزهت وكمل نماؤها نجت من العاهة وقل الخطر فيها، وليس كذلك إذا يبست الأصول، فإنها قد انتهى نماؤها ونجت من العاهة، فكان ذلك بمنزلة أن تزهي.
B - 2 - Dropping an effective description in the ruling attached to the cause. Example: The Shafiʿi says about selling what the buyer has not seen: It is a sale of something with unknown description at the time of contract, so its sale is invalid, just as if he said 'I sold you a garment'. The opponent says: This is broken by the case where a man marries a woman he has not seen, for she is of unknown description at the time of contract, and then the marriage is valid. The proponent answers: Marriage is not like sale in this ruling because ignorance has an effect in the chapter of sale that it does not have in marriage. For this reason, if a man marries a woman he has not seen and then sees her, no option (khiyar) is established; but if he buys a commodity he has not seen and then sees it, the option is established. So it is possible that the lack of vision in marriage does not prevent validity, while in sale it does prevent validity. Another example: The Maliki argues that the sale of fruit before its ripeness (badu al-salah) is absolutely impermissible, because it is a growing fruit sold before ripeness without a condition of cutting, so it must be invalid, just as if the condition of leaving (tabqiya) were stipulated. The Hanafi says: Not so, because [the sale] is singled out before ripeness without a condition of cutting, and if it is sold as if the fruits had dried up, the opponent [argues] that he has dropped the description effective for the ruling. So this kasr is not valid because the description is effective. The way of answer for the proponent is to show the invalidity of this type of kasr, and then [explain] that growth with the condition of leaving is risk (gharar) and exposure to blight, therefore the Prophet ﷺ forbade the sale of fruits until they become ripe, i.e., red, and until they are safe from blight. When they ripen and complete their growth, they are safe from blight and the risk is reduced. That is not the case when the fruits have dried up, for their growth has ended and they are safe from blight, so that is like the state of ripening.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of objection in jadal: dropping an effective description from the cause. The author gives two fiqh examples. In the first, the Shafiʿi argues that selling an unseen item is invalid due to ignorance of description; the opponent counters with marriage of an unseen woman being valid. The Shafiʿi responds by distinguishing the effectiveness of ignorance in sale versus marriage, using the option (khiyar) as evidence. The author then provides a second example: the Maliki argues that selling fruit before ripeness is invalid, analogous to selling with a condition to leave; the Hanafi objects that the Maliki has dropped the description of growth. The Maliki responds by showing that growth with the condition of leaving involves risk (gharar) and exposure to blight, and that the prophetic prohibition on selling fruit before ripening is based on this risk. Therefore, the descriptions are not equally effective. The commentary explains that the correct response is to demonstrate that the dropped description is not equally effective in the two cases.

نكاح
خيار
غرر
بدو الصلاح

A) Because marriage is more important in Islamic law.

C) Because ignorance is always more harmful in marriage.
D) Because the woman is not considered a commodity.

Key Takeaway
The objection of Isqat Wasf Mu'aththir fails unless the description is equally effective in both original and counterexample; a proper response demonstrates contextual differences in effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a jadal exchange on the following question: Is the sale of a car without a test drive valid? Use the objection of Isqat Wasf Mu'aththir by comparing to marriage of an unseen woman. Objection: Cause Does Not Run in Its Effects
Author explains the objection that the cause does not apply uniformly, with two fiqh examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the objection that a cause does not run in its effects and construct a valid response by differentiating between rulings, using examples from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Consistency test: if a cause produces one effect, it must produce similar effects or be invalid.

In jadal, after a proponent introduces a cause for a ruling, the objector may demand that the cause be applied uniformly to similar rulings. This is the fourth type of objection: the cause does not run in its effects. Understanding how to respond by distinguishing between the rulings is essential for defending legal analogies in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and cause
Find where the proponent (e.g., Hanafi) states that a cause (ʿilla) yields a specific ruling. The cause is often presented as a general principle applied to a case.
In the first example: the Hanafi claims that fasting Ramadan does not require intention because it is a specific obligation (like returning a deposit).
2
Extract the objector's demand for consistency
The objector (Shafiʿi) argues that if the cause were valid, it should also produce a similar effect in a related ruling. The objector demands that the same cause be tested on a parallel case.
The Shafiʿi says: If specificity drops specification, it should also drop intention, because specification necessitates intention.
3
Analyze the proponent's response: differentiation
The proponent must show that the two rulings are fundamentally different in their underlying reasons (maʿna). The cause does not apply uniformly because the rulings serve different purposes.
The Hanafi distinguishes: intention is for devotion, specification is for distinguishing types; time may accommodate devotion but not types, so only intention is needed.
4
Apply the pattern to the second example
Repeat steps 1-3 for the second example (mukatib). The Hanafi claims tithe is obligatory; the Maliki objects that the same cause would require zakat, which is absent. The response should show that the rulings differ.
The passage does not give a response for the second example; students can infer that the Hanafi might argue that zakat and tithe are different in their conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على العلة بأنها لا تجري في معلولاتها وهذا الاعتراض قريب من معنى الكسر ، ومفاده أن يعلّق المستدل على العلة حكما ما، فيطالبه المعترض بأن يعلّق على تلك العلة ما يشاكل ذلك الحكم، ويقول له : لو كان علة في أحد الحكمين، لكان علة في الآخر. مثال ذلك أن يقول حنفي: إن صوم رمضان لا يفتقر إلى تعيين النية لأنه مستحق العين فهو كرد الوديعة، فيقول شافعي معترضا: استحقاق العين كما يوجب إسقاط العين والتعيين يوجب إسقاط النية ، فلو أسقط التعيين لأسقط النية، والجواب : أن يبين المستدل اختلاف الحكمين وذلك أن يقول : النية تراد لتحصيل القربة والزمان يحتمل القربة وغير القربة فافتقر إلى النية لتحصيل القربة، والتعيين يراد للتمييز بين أصناف القرب والزمان لا يحتمل أصناف القرب، ولهذا المعنى افتقر طواف الزيارة إلى النية لتحصيل القربة ثم لا يفتقر إلى التعيين لأن الوقت لا يحتمل أصناف القرب. ومثال ذلك أيضًا؛ أن يقول حنفي : إن العشر واجب على المكاتب، لأن كل من وجب عليه كراء المزدرع وجب عليه عشر الزرع كالحر المسلم، فيقول مالكي معترضا : لو كان هذا علة في وجوب العشر في زرعه لكان علة في وجوب الزكاة في ماله لأنهما يجريان مجرى واحدًا ويصرفان إلى وجه واحد، ومن وجب عليه أحدهما وجب عليه الآخر، كالحر المسلم، فلما رأينا المكاتب لا زكاة عليه في ماله علمنا أنه لا عشر عليه في زرعه.
Fourth topic: Objection against the cause that it does not run in its effects. This objection is close in meaning to the kasr. Its gist is that the proponent attaches a ruling to the cause, and the objector demands that he attach to that cause what resembles that ruling, saying: If it were a cause in one of the two rulings, it would be a cause in the other. An example: A Hanafi says: The fast of Ramadan does not require specification of intention because it is a specific obligation, like returning a deposit. A Shafiʿi objects: Specificity (istihqaq al-ʿayn) necessitates dropping the specification, and specification (taʿyin) necessitates dropping the intention. So if it drops specification, it would drop intention. The answer: The proponent must show the difference between the two rulings, saying: Intention is sought to achieve devotion (qurba), and time may accommodate devotion and non-devotion, so intention is needed to achieve devotion. Specification is sought to distinguish between types of acts of devotion, and time does not accommodate types of devotion. For this reason, the circumambulation of visitation (tawaf al-ziyara) requires intention to achieve devotion but does not require specification because the time does not accommodate types of devotion. Another example: A Hanafi says: The tithe (ʿushr) is obligatory on a mukatib (slave in contract of manumission), because every person on whom the rent of the cultivated land is obligatory, the tithe of the crop is obligatory on him, like a free Muslim. A Maliki objects: If this were a cause for the obligation of tithe on his crop, it would be a cause for the obligation of zakat on his wealth, because both run in the same vein and are directed to the same purpose. And whoever is obligated to one is obligated to the other, like a free Muslim. But when we see that the mukatib has no zakat on his wealth, we know that he has no tithe on his crop.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth type of objection in jadal: objecting that the cause does not apply uniformly to its effects. The author first explains that this is similar to kasr: the objector demands that if the cause is valid for one ruling, it should apply to a similar ruling. Two examples are given. In the first, the Hanafi argues that fasting Ramadan does not require specifying intention because it is a specific obligation; the Shafiʿi objects that if specificity drops specification, it should also drop intention. The Hanafi responds by distinguishing between the purpose of intention (to achieve devotion) and specification (to distinguish types). The second example involves the Hanafi claiming tithe on a mukatib; the Maliki objects that the same cause would require zakat on his wealth, but since it is absent, the cause is invalid. The passage teaches the method of showing that the two rulings are different in their underlying reasons.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
مكاتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, when the Maliki objects to the Hanafi's claim about the mukatib, what is his argument?
A) The cause for tithe does not apply to zakat because they are different rulings.
B) If the cause obligates tithe, it should also obligate zakat, but since it doesn't, the cause is invalid.
C) The mukatib is not obligated to pay zakat because he is a slave.
D) The Hanafi's analogy with a free Muslim is flawed.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the cause does not run in its effects tests the consistency of a cause across similar rulings, and the proponent can respond by showing that the rulings differ in their underlying reasons.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the structure of the objection for the second example (mukatib): state the Hanafi's cause, the Maliki's objection demanding consistency, and then propose a possible response by distinguishing the rulings (e.g., zakat vs. tithe have different conditions).
?
Consider a new case: A Hanafi argues that the 'idda of a divorced woman is three menstrual cycles because of the possibility of pregnancy. A Shafi'i objects: If that were the cause, then the 'idda of a widow would also be three cycles, but it is four months and ten days. How should the Hanafi respond to show the rulings differ? (Hint: widow's 'idda includes mourning period.)

Definition and Types of Fahwa al-Khitab
Defines fahwa al-khitab and distinguishes types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define fahwa al-khitab (mafhum al-muwafaqa), identify its condition of 'awla', and distinguish between the jali (clear) and khafi (hidden) types with Quranic and juristic examples.

Core Idea
Fahwa al-khitab infers a ruling for the unspoken case that is either clear and certain or probable and open to ijtihad.

In this lesson, we explore a fundamental tool of Islamic legal reasoning: fahwa al-khitab, also known as mafhum al-muwafaqa. This is an a fortiori argument where the ruling on an unspoken case is derived because it is more deserving of the ruling than the spoken case. The passage distinguishes between two types: jali (clear, certain) and khafi (hidden, probable). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating when such arguments yield definitive rulings versus when they enter the realm of ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the two scholarly positions on fahwa al-khitab. The first defines it as any agreement between spoken and unspoken rulings. The second, from al-Tilimsani, adds the condition that the unspoken must be more deserving (awla) of the ruling.
The second definition is more restrictive and focuses on a fortiori reasoning.
2
Extract the Quranic examples
Read the examples from Surat al-Isra' (prohibition of 'uff') and Surat al-Zalzala (atom's weight). Understand how each shows that the unspoken case (hitting, greater deeds) is more deserving of the ruling.
3
Compare jali and khafi
Notice the criteria: jali (clear) is certain because the suitability is obvious; khafi (hidden) is probable because even though the branch seems more deserving, there is room for ijtihad.
The Maliki example about making up prayer is classified as khafi.
4
Apply the distinction
Test your understanding: Given a new example, determine whether it falls under jali or khafi based on the clarity of the a fortiori connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توطئة : المبحث الرابع : الجدل فى الاستدلال بفحوى الخطاب . فحوى الخطاب يسمى أيضًا مفهوم الموافقة، وهو - عند بعض الأصوليين ما يدل على أن الحكم في المسكوت عنه موافق للحكم في المنطوق . ورأى بعض آخر من علماء أصول الفقه أن من شرط الحكم بمفهوم الموافقة أن يكون المسكوت عنه أولى بالحكم من المنطوق به، فقال التلمساني مثلا تعريفه : «أن يعلم أن المسكوت عنه أولى بالحكم من المنطوق به» فالمفهوم تارة يكون أولى بالحكم من المنطوق، كدلالة تحريم التأفف : تعالى (فَلَا تَقُل لَّهُمَا أُفٍّ) على تحريم الضرب وكل أنواع الأذى الذي هو أبلغ من التأفف ، ذلك قوله تعالى : (فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ وَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ شَرًّا يَرَهُ) ، فإذا كان مثقال ذرة من العمل يؤجر عليه الإنسان إن كان خيرًا ويوزر عليه إن كان شرا، فإن ما زاد على ذلك أولى بأن يؤجر عليه الإنسان أو يوزر . وهذا النوع إما أن يكون جليًا أو يكون خفيًا . فالجلي هو القطعي إذ تظهر فيه مناسبة الحكم للفرع واضحة جدا، وذلك كما في الأمثلة السابقة . والخفي هو الظني الذي وإن كان يبدو الفرع فيه أولى بالحكم من الأصل، إلا أنه يبقى محتملا وواقعا في محل الاجتهاد، كما في قول المالكية في أن تارك الصلاة متعمدا مطالب بقضائها وجوبا بقوله : (من نام عن صلاة أو نسيها فليصلها إذا ذكرها) ، قالوا : فإذا كان النائم والساهي يقضيان الصلاة وهما غير متعمدين فالمتعمد أولى بالقضاء.
Prolegomenon: Fourth Section: Dialectic in reasoning by way of the import of speech (fahwa al-khitab). The import of speech is also called coincident meaning (mafhum al-muwafaqa). It is – according to some usulis – that which indicates that the ruling concerning the unspoken case coincides with the ruling concerning the spoken case. Some other scholars of usul al-fiqh held that among the conditions for ruling by coincident meaning is that the unspoken case is more deserving of the ruling than the spoken case. For example, al-Tilimsani defined it as: 'That one knows that the unspoken case is more deserving of the ruling than the spoken case.' Sometimes the meaning (mafhum) is more deserving of the ruling than the spoken, such as the indication of the prohibition of saying 'uff' – He Most High said: 'So do not say to them uff' – on the prohibition of hitting and all types of harm which are more severe than saying uff. And that is from His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it, and whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it.' For if an atom's weight of a deed is something for which a person is rewarded if it is good and punished if it is evil, then what is more than that is more deserving that the person be rewarded or punished for it. This type is either clear (jali) or hidden (khafi). The clear is certain (qat'i), since the suitability of the ruling for the branch appears very clearly, as in the previous examples. The hidden is probable (zanni), for even though the branch appears more deserving of the ruling than the original, it still remains possible and falls into the sphere of ijtihad, as in the Maliki view that the one who intentionally abandons prayer is required to make it up obligatorily, based on his saying: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it.' They said: If the sleeping and the forgetful person make up the prayer while they are not intentional, then the intentional person is more deserving of making it up.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of a key concept in dialectical reasoning: 'fahwa al-khitab' (the import/implication of speech), also known as 'mafhum al-muwafaqa' (coincident meaning or a fortiori argument). The author first presents two scholarly positions on its definition. The first group of usulis (scholars of legal theory) defines it broadly as any case where the ruling on the unspoken (maskan 'anhu) matches the ruling on the spoken (mantiq bihi). The second group, represented here by al-Tilimsani, adds a condition: the unspoken must be more deserving (awla) of the ruling than the spoken. This second definition focuses on the a fortiori aspect. The author then illustrates with two Quranic examples. The first is from Surat al-Isra' (17:23): the prohibition of saying 'uff' to parents implies the prohibition of hitting them, because hitting is more severe than an expression of annoyance. The second is from Surat al-Zalzala (99:7-8): an atom's weight of good or evil brings reward or punishment; therefore a greater amount is even more deserving of recompense. The passage then divides this type into two categories: clear (jali) and hidden (khafi). The clear is certain (qat'i) because the suitability of the ruling for the branch is obvious. The hidden is probable (zanni) because even though the branch seems more deserving, it remains within the realm of ijtihad. The Maliki example is given: the hadith about the sleeping or forgetful person making up prayer is used to argue that the intentional abandoner is even more deserving of making up prayer, but this is probabilistic, not certain.

Key Terms
فحوى الخطاب
مفهوم الموافقة
منطوق
مسكوت عنه
جلي
خفي
قطعي
ظني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of the clear (jali) type of fahwa al-khitab?
A) Deriving the obligation to make up prayer for the intentional abandoner from the hadith about the sleeping person.
B) Deriving the prohibition of hitting parents from the prohibition of saying 'uff' to them.
C) Deriving that the intentional abandoner of prayer is more deserving of punishment than the forgetful one.
D) Deriving that an atom's weight of good is rewarded, so a greater amount is also rewarded.

Key Takeaway
Fahwa al-khitab is a type of a fortiori argument that can be certain (jali) or probable (khafi), depending on the clarity of the suitability of the ruling for the unspoken case.

Parsing a Conditional Legal Argument in Usul al-Fiqh
Author presents a conditional argument about hadd punishment in zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent, consequent, and 'illa in a conditional legal argument from usul al-fiqqh.

Core Idea
A conditional argument in fiqh links a legal ruling to a rational cause.

In this passage, the author presents a conditional argument against applying the hadd punishment for zina in a specific scenario. The argument uses a conditional structure ('لوجب ألا يجب') and provides a causal explanation ('لأنه ليس فيه خلط النسب ولا إفساد الفراش'). By parsing this argument, you will learn how jurists deploy conditional reasoning to question or refine legal rulings. This is a typical dialectical move in usul al-fiqh discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional structure
Look for the phrase 'لوجب ألا يجب' (it would be necessary that … not obligatory). This signals a conditional consequence: if a certain condition holds, then the ruling changes.
The conditional here is implicit in the wording; the antecedent is the cause introduced by 'لأنه' (because).
2
Extract the antecedent and consequent
Antecedent: the act of zina with a woman who has no husband does not involve mixing of lineage nor corruption of the bed. Consequent: the hadd punishment is not obligatory. The logical form is: If (no khalṭ al-nasab and no ifsād al-firāsh) then (hadd not obligatory).
The antecedent is the absence of two specific harms, which function as the 'illa (cause) for the ruling.
3
Identify the 'illa (cause)
The 'illa is the rationale behind the ruling: the preservation of lineage and marital integrity. Here, the absence of these harms is the reason for negating the hadd. In usul al-fiqh, the 'illa is the effective cause that justifies a legal ruling.
This is a typical 'illa extracted from the wisdom (ḥikma) of the punishment.
4
Place the argument in a dialectical context
This conditional likely serves as an objection (mu'tarid) in a jadal debate. A claimant might assert that hadd is obligatory for all zina. The objector uses this conditional to show that in cases lacking the 'illa, the ruling does not apply.
The argument does not stand alone; it is part of a larger back-and-forth where the opponent must respond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجب ألا يجب الحد في الزنا بوطء من لا زوج لها لأنه ليس فيه خلط النسب ولا إفساد الفراش
It would be necessary that the hadd punishment is not obligatory in zina by intercourse with one who has no husband because it does not involve mixing of lineage nor corruption of the bed.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage presents a conditional legal argument. It posits that if a certain condition is absent (mixing of lineage or corruption of the bed), then the hadd punishment for zina should not apply. The argument uses a conditional structure: 'لوجب ألا يجب الحد' (it would be necessary that the hadd is not obligatory) indicating a hypothetical consequence. The reason given is the absence of two harms: mixing of lineage and corruption of the bed. This is a typical juristic reasoning pattern in usul al-fiqh, often used in objections or refinements of rulings. The student should identify the conditional form and the underlying 'illa (cause).

Key Terms
الحد
الزنا
خلط النسب
إفساد الفراش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Conditional arguments in fiqh link legal rulings to their underlying causes (علل), enabling jurists to refine or challenge general rules.

Adab al-Jadal: Sincerity, Humility, and Intellectual Flexibility
Lists etiquettes for the disputant's attitude towards truth and the opponent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the key etiquettes (adab) of jadal mentioned in this passage and identify their role in ensuring a productive dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
The adab of jadal align the disputant's soul with truth-seeking.

Before engaging in debate, a mujtahid must cultivate certain inner states. This passage lists essential adab for jadal. Each item is a prerequisite for sincere truth-seeking and respectful disagreement. Let's parse them carefully.

Guided Path
1
Identify each adab in the Arabic text
Look at the Arabic list: 'الحق والتقرب به إلى الله، التواضع المرونة الفكرية والاستعداد لتقبل الحق عند ظهوره، التمييز بين مراتب من يجادلهم، عدم التسامح مع المخالف فيما يتعلق بالحق، التيقظ لكلام المخالف وتفهمه جيدا التخلي عن التعصب لوجهة النظر السابقة'. Count how many distinct adab appear. Note any punctuation that separates them.
The passage appears to list seven adab, though the final phrase is incomplete.
2
Translate each adab precisely
For each adab, produce a translation that captures its technical meaning in jadal. For example, 'الحق والتقرب به إلى الله' means seeking truth for closeness to Allah. Check the literal translation provided.
Some adab may have broader meanings; focus on the jadal context.
3
Categorize each adab as internal state or external action
Decide whether each adab concerns the disputant's inner disposition (e.g., humility, sincerity) or their outward interaction with the opponent (e.g., understanding the opponent's speech, distinguishing ranks).
Several are both, but try to assign a primary category.
4
Explain how each adab contributes to the goal of jadal
The goal of jadal is to establish truth for the sake of Allah. For each adab, articulate how violating it would derail that goal. For example, without humility, one might resist evidence.
Refer to the common misunderstanding that adab are just politeness.

The Six Objections to Qur'anic Evidence in Jadal
Enumerates six specific objections to Quranic evidence in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify each of the six types of objections against istidlal by the Qur'an in an usuli debate.

Core Idea
Six distinct avenues exist to challenge a Qur'anic argument; each targets a different layer of evidence.

In usuli jadal, the opponent does not simply reject the Qur'an; he must engage with it. Scholars identified six principled ways to object to an istidlal bi-l-kitab. Mastering these categories equips you to anticipate and respond to objections in theological and legal debate.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the six categories
Internalize the six types: (1) disputing the verse's indication according to the arguer's own usul, (2) conceding the import but disputing the practical implication, (3) extracting a different meaning from the same evidence, (4) differences in qira'at, (5) abrogation, and (6) ta'wil.
These categories cover nearly all principled objections.
2
Distinguish between objections to dalala and muqtada
Types 1 and 2 both accept the verse itself but differ: type 1 attacks the verse's indication (dalala) per the arguer's usul, while type 2 attacks its practical implication (muqtada). This distinction is crucial for proper classification.
3
Recognize objections based on alternative texts or interpretations
Types 3-6 involve alternative textual sources: same verse but different meaning (3), different qira'a (4), abrogation (5), or alternative ta'wil (6). Learn to identify which dimension is being contested.
Abrogation and ta'wil are common in fiqh debates.
4
Apply the categories to a real debate scenario
Given a hypothetical objection from a juristic debate, determine which of the six types it matches. This develops practical classification skill for actual munazara.
Practice with examples from ikhtilaf al-fuqaha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
26 - أما الاعتراضات التي ترد على الاستدلال بالكتاب فهي ترجع إلى ستة أنواع : - فهي إما اعتراضات من جهة أن المستدل بالآية القرآنية على معنى ما لا يقول بدلالتها أصوليا على ذلك المعنى . - أو اعتراضات بالتسليم بموجب الآية أصوليا لكن مع المنازعة في مقتضاها الذي تدل عليه عمليا. - أو اعتراضات من جهة أن الدليل الذي أخذ به المستدل يأخذ به المعترض كذلك ولكن يستخرج منه معنى آخر غير المعنى الذي استخرجه المستدل. . أو اعتراضات من جهة اختلاف القراءات حيث يأخذ المستدل بالآية على قراءة ما، ويأخذ المعترض بالآية نفسها لكن على قراءة ثانية قد تفيد معنى غير معنى الأولى. - أو اعتراضات من جهة النسخ، حيث يستدل المدعي بآية ما، فيعترضه المخالف بأنها منسوخة أو تتضمن معنى منسوخا . - أو اعتراضات من جهة التأويل، حيث يستدل المدعي بآية لمعنى ما، فيؤول المعترض الآية لمعنى آخر مباين .
26 - As for the objections that are made against istidlal by the Book, they reduce to six types: (1) They are either objections from the aspect that the one using a Quranic verse for a certain meaning does not, according to usul, hold that it indicates that meaning. (2) Or objections by conceding the import of the verse on usul grounds but disputing its implication that it indicates practically. (3) Or objections from the aspect that the evidence which the arguer took, the objector also takes it, but he extracts from it another meaning different from the meaning the arguer extracted. (4) Or objections from the aspect of differences in readings (qira'at), where the arguer takes the verse according to one reading, and the objector takes the same verse but according to a second reading that may yield a meaning different from the first. (5) Or objections from the aspect of abrogation (naskh), where the claimant adduces a verse, and the opponent objects that it is abrogated or contains an abrogated meaning. (6) Or objections from the aspect of interpretation (ta'wil), where the claimant adduces a verse for a certain meaning, and the objector interprets the verse to another contrary meaning.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage lists six types of objections that can be raised against arguments based on the Qur'an in usuli jadal. Each type targets a different aspect of the istidlal: (1) The verse may not be considered by the arguer himself as evidence for the claimed meaning in his own usul (i.e., inconsistency). (2) The objector concedes the verse's literal import but disputes its practical implication. (3) Both parties accept the same evidence but the objector derives a different meaning from it. (4) Variant readings (qira'at) of the same verse yield different meanings, allowing objection by adopting an alternative reading. (5) The verse may be abrogated (mansukh) or its meaning abrogated, thus no longer valid. (6) The objector reinterprets (ta'wil) the verse to a meaning incompatible with the arguer's claim. These categories cover the main ways a Qur'anic argument can be challenged in the usuli tradition.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بالكتاب
المنازعة في المقتضى
اختلاف القراءات
النسخ
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of objection involves the arguer and objector using the same Quranic verse but deriving different meanings?
A) Objection from abrogation
B) Objection from interpretation (ta'wil)
C) Objection from differences in readings
D) Objection from conceding the import but disputing the implication

Key Takeaway
The six objections provide a systematic framework for challenging Qur'anic evidence in jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each objection: (a) 'Your verse has been abrogated by a later revelation.' (b) 'The verse you cite means something else in another qira'a.'
?
Construct your own objection of type 1 (inconsistency) to a specific verse used as evidence in a fiqh ruling, explaining why the arguer's own usul would reject the indication.
